(قَوْلُهُ : بِالضَّرُورَةِ . . . إِلَخ الباءُ لِلملابسة؛ أي: انقساماً ملتبساً بالضرورة، ثمَّ يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالضَّرورة البداهة، وأن يكون المراد بها التَّطْعَ، والظَّاهِرُ الأول، وحينئذ؛ فقولُ الشَّارح : وإِنَّما كان تقسيم ... إلخ، من باب التنبيه لا من
العطار
وما في التسهيل من أنَّ افتعل يكون بمعنى فعل، وفسَّرَ الدَّوَّاني الاقتسام بالأخذ؛ حيث قال : أي يأخذُ كلٌّ مِنَ التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ قِسماً من الضرورة والاكتساب؛ أي : الضَّروريّ والمكتسب .ا.هـ. فالضَّرورة والاكتساب مفعولان، قال العصام : وهوَ نِعْمَ التَّوجيه لو ساعده اللُّغة، ولم نجد في كتبِ اللُّغةِ أنَّ الافتعال يجي للاتِّخاذ، وقد يوجه بأنَّ المرادَ : يَقتسمان بينهما، ويلزمه أنْ يأخذ كلَّ مِنْهُمَا قِسماً ؛ انتهى. وأُجيب بثبوتِ مجيء الافتعال بمعنى الأخذ؛ نحو: ارتفق زيداً: أي: اتَّخَذَهُ رفيقاً، وهناك احتمال ثالث مبني على مجيء اقتسم بمعنى تقاسم؛ فقد ذكر التّسهيل مِن معاني افتعل تفاعل؛ فيقرأ يقتسمان بالبناء للفاعل، والضَّرورة والنَّظرُ على ظَاهرِهما مِن غير تأويل؛ مفعولان ليقتسمان، والمعنى حينئذ يأخذُ التَّصوُّرُ قِسماً من الضَّرورةِ؛ فيتحقَّقُ قسم ضروري، وقسماً مِنَ الاكتساب؛ فيتحقَّقُ تَصوُّرٌ مُكتسب، ويُقالُ مثله في التصديق ؛ فهو على حد قول الشاعر :
في كما صنع إنَّا اقتَسَمْنَا خُطَّتَيْنَا بِينَنَا فَحَمَلْتُ بَرَّةً واحتمَلتَ فَجار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : التَّصَوُّرُ والتَّصْدِيقُ) تقديمُ التَّصوُّرِ هنا على التصديق دون العكس المصنِّفُ؛ لأنَّ المنظور إليهِ هَهُنا ماصدقهما وأفرادهما، فإنَّهُ الَّذي يوصفُ بالضَّرورةِ والكَسْبِ ، وأمَّا مفهومُهُما ؛ فهو نظري كما سيأتي، قالَ شَارِحُ سُلَّم العلوم: ليس بين الضَّروري والكسبي تقابلُ الإيجابِ والسَّلبِ؛ لأنَّ المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسَّلبِ لا يخلو موضوع ما عنهُما ، وإلا ؛ لزم ارتفاعُ النَّقيضين، والموجوداتُ الغيبيَّةُ كُلُّها خالية عنهما، ولا التّضايفُ، وهو ظاهر، بل إنَّما يتصوَّرُ التّضادُّ أو العدمُ والمَلَكةُ، ولا بُدَّ في التَّضادّ من إمكان تعاقبهما على موضوع
Introduction of the author: (And they are divided) 
Dasuqi: (His statement: And they are divided ... etc.) is a start of what is a prelude for the need, and the need is the preservation of the mind from error in thinking referred to by his statement: 'So there is need for a rule ... etc.' 
Attar: (His statement: And they are divided) The reason for delaying this division after the one before it has preceded. Then there are two copies: The first: 'yanqasimani' from the passive form, and he mentioned the preposition 'ila' in his statement 'al-darura', and upon it the commentator wrote, and it is apparent, needing only that by 'darura' and 'iktisab' is meant the necessary and the acquired; because they are the two divisions of conception and assent. The commentator indicated this by his statement: 'And indeed the division was ... etc.' Also, the thing divided is carried upon the division in dividing the universal into its particulars as here, and without interpretation the carrying is not sound. It may be correct to leave it without interpretation based on placing the term 'division' in place of 'the divided'; like dividing animal into rational and neighing, so the division here is knowledge with necessity and knowledge with acquisition, meaning necessary and acquired. The second: What al-Jalal al-Dawani and al-Isam wrote upon, which is: 'yaqtasimani' from the ifti'al form, and the preposition 'ila' is omitted, and it is possible to read it as active voice, and 'al-darura' and 'al-iktisab' meaning the necessary and the acquired ... etc., as we said. An objection to this possibility is that it necessitates dividing the necessary and theoretical into conception and assent; because the meaning then is that conception and assent divide the necessary and acquired; i.e., each of them obtains a portion of the necessary and acquired; thus each of the necessary and acquired becomes divided, and the assumed is the opposite; namely that the divided is conception and assent, not that they are two divisions. And that it be read as passive, and 'al-darura' and 'al-iktisab' are in the accusative by removal of the preposition, and in them is the interpretation we mentioned. What is objected to this—that the accusative by removal of the preposition is restricted to hearing—is answered by that it has become frequent in their speech until it is considered among allowances. These two possibilities are based on what was mentioned in al-Asas that 'qasamahu' and 'iqtasamahu' are synonymous. 
(1) (His statement: two copies) Thus in the copy in our possession, but the correct is 'nuskhatayni' in the accusative because it is the noun of 'inna' delayed after its predicate, the adverb. End. al-Sharnubi. 
Meaning: conception and assent (by necessity); 
Dasuqi: (His statement: by necessity ... etc.) The preposition 'ba' is for accompaniment; i.e., a division accompanied by necessity. Then it is possible that by 'darura' is meant self-evidence, and that by it is meant certainty, and the apparent is the first. Then his statement: 'And indeed the division was ... etc.' is of the type of alerting, not of ... 
Attar: And what is in al-Tashil that 'ifta'ala' can have the meaning of 'fa'ala', and al-Dawani interpreted 'iqtisam' as taking; where he said: i.e., each of conception and assent takes a portion of necessity and acquisition; i.e., the necessary and the acquired. End. So 'al-darura' and 'al-iktisab' are objects. al-Isam said: It is an excellent interpretation if language supported it, and we do not find in books of language that 'ifti'al' comes for taking. It may be interpreted that the meaning is they divide between themselves, and it follows that each of them takes a portion; end. It was answered by the proof that 'ifti'al' comes with the meaning of taking; like 'irtadaqa Zaydan' i.e., made him a companion. There is a third possibility based on 'iqtasama' coming with the meaning of 'taqasama'; for al-Tashil mentions among the meanings of 'ifta'ala' is 'tafa'ala'; so it is read 'yaqtasimani' as active voice, and 'al-darura' and 'al-nazar' are on their apparent meaning without interpretation, as objects of 'yaqtasimani', and the meaning then is that conception takes a portion of necessity, so a necessary division is realized, and a portion of acquisition, so an acquired conception is realized, and the same is said concerning assent; it is like the poet's saying: 
'Indeed we divided our two lines between us, so I carried the good and you carried the wicked.' 
(His statement: i.e., conception and assent) Here he gives precedence to conception over assent, unlike the author's reverse, because what is looked at here is their referents and individuals, for they are described by necessity and acquisition. As for their concept, it is theoretical as will come. The commentator on Sullam al-Ulum said: There is not between the necessary and the acquired an opposition of affirmation and negation; because the two opposites by affirmation and negation do not have a subject devoid of both, otherwise the removal of the two contradictories would be necessary, and all unseen existences are devoid of them. Nor is it correlativity, which is apparent. Rather only contrariety or privation and possession can be imagined, and in contrariety there must be the possibility of them alternating upon a subject.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's statement 'And they are divided' (wa yanqasimani), referring to tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). The commentator, using the works of Dasuqi and Attar, explains the division of these two into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). The passage begins by identifying the text as the author's introduction, and then discusses the textual variants in the wording. There are two manuscript readings: one with the passive verb 'yanqasimani' (from infi'al) including the preposition 'ila', and another with the active verb 'yaqtasimani' (from ifti'al) without the preposition. The commentator analyzes each reading, noting the need for interpretation. The first reading, 'yanqasimani bi-d-darura', is taken as 'they are divided by necessity', where 'darura' means the necessary. The second reading, 'yaqtasimani', means 'they take portions', and the commentator objects that this would imply dividing necessary and theoretical into conception and assent, which reverses the intended division. The discussion includes grammatical points, such as the accusative case by removal of a preposition, and references to linguistic works like al-Asas and al-Tashil. A third possibility is that 'yaqtasimani' has the meaning of mutual division, as in the poet's verse. The passage then explains the meaning of 'bi-d-darura', noting that the preposition 'ba' indicates accompaniment, and 'darura' could mean self-evidence or certainty, with the first being more apparent. Finally, the passage addresses the order of mentioning conception before assent, and quotes a commentator on Sullam al-Ulum to clarify that the relationship between necessary and acquired is not that of contradictory opposites, but rather of contrariety or privation and possession. Throughout, the commentator emphasizes the precise wording and its implications for understanding the epistemological division. The passage is a detailed grammatical and logical analysis, typical of a sharh that expands on a compact matn statement.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to reading 'yaqtasimani' as active voice in the second manuscript?
A) It would imply the necessary and acquired are the divisions, not conception and assent.
B) It would make the verb transitive without an object.
C) It would contradict the passive form of the first reading.
D) It would require a preposition that is missing.

Key Takeaway
The choice between 'yanqasimani' and 'yaqtasimani' crucially determines whether conception and assent are the divided or the divisions, requiring careful grammatical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare the two readings: 'yanqasimani ila al-darura' and 'yaqtasimani al-darura'. Explain how each reading affects the logical direction of division.
?
Given a manuscript variant in Usul al-Fiqh where the verb 'tataqassamu' (they are subdivided) is spelled 'tatafassamu', apply the same grammatical analysis to determine the intended meaning.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
The Logical Relationship Between Necessary and Acquired
Commentator clarifies the logical relationship between necessary and acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of logical opposition (contrariety or privation/possession) that holds between the necessary (daruri) and the acquired (kasbi), and distinguish it from contradictory opposition.

Core Idea
Necessary and acquired are not contradictories but contraries or privation/possession.

A commentator on Sullam al-Ulum clarifies that the relationship between the necessary and the acquired is not one of affirmation and negation (contradiction), but rather of contrariety or privation and possession. This means that not every subject must be either necessary or acquired; some things (like unseen existences) are neither. Understanding this nuance prevents logical errors in defining the scope of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the proposed logical relationship
The commentator says there is no opposition of affirmation and negation (contradiction) between necessary and acquired.
Contradiction: one true, the other false, with no middle.
2
Identify why contradiction fails
Because there are subjects that are neither necessary nor acquired, e.g., unseen existences (ghaybiyyat) which are beyond human knowledge.
This avoids the problem of 'both false' (removal of contradictories).
3
Determine the correct type of opposition
The relationship is either contrariety (taaddud) or privation and possession (adam wa malaka).
Both allow for a middle ground.
4
Check the condition for contrariety
For contrariety, the two can alternate on the same subject, meaning a subject can change from necessary to acquired or vice versa over time.
This is plausible for knowledge: what is acquired for one may become self-evident for another.

Source Text

مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتنِ (وَيَنْقَسِمَانِ) ؛
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَيَنْقَسِمَانِ ... إلخ) شروعٌ فيما هو تمهيد للحاجة، والحاجة عصمة الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر المشارِ لهُ بقولِهِ : فاحتيج إلى قانون . . . إلخ.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : وَيَنْقَسِمَانِ) تقدَّمَ وجه تأخير هذا التَّقسيم عن الَّذي قبله، ثمَّ إِنَّ هَهُنا نُسختان (۱) ؛ الأُولى: ينقسمان من باب الانفعال، وذكر إلى الجارة في قوله : الضَّرورة، وعليها كتب الشارح، وهي ظاهرة لا تحتاج إلا لأن يرادَ مِنَ الضَّرورةِ والاكتسابِ الضَّروري والمكتسب ؛ لأنَّهما القِسمانِ مِنَ التَّصورِ والتَّصديق، وقد أشارَ لِذلكَ الشَّارحُ بقوله : وإِنَّما كانَ تقسيم ... إلخ، وأيضاً المقسم يحمل على القسم في تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئيَّاتِهِ كما هنا، وبدونِ التَّأويل لا يستقيمُ الحَمْلُ، وقد يصح إبقاؤها بلا تأويل ؛ بناءً على وضع قيدِ القسم مكانَ القسم؛ كَتَقسيم الحيوانِ إلى النَّاطِقِ والصَّاهِل، فالقِسمُ هَهُنا علمٌ ذو ضرورةٍ، وعلم ذو كسب، وهو معنى ضروري وكسبي، الثانية: ما كتب عليها الجلالُ الدَّوَانيُّ والعصامُ، وهي: ويقتسمان من باب الافتعال وحذف إلى الجارة، وهي محتملة لأن يقرأ بالبناء للفاعل، والضَّرورة والاكتساب بمعنى الضَّروريّ والمكتسب . . . إلخ، ما قلنا، ويرد على هذا الاحتمال اقتضاؤُهُ تقسيم الضَّروريّ والنَّظري إلى تَصوُّرٍ وتَصديقٍ؛ لأنَّ المعنى حينئذٍ يقسمُ التّصور والتصديق الضَّروري والمكتسب؛ أي: يحصل كلّ منهما قسماً من الضَّروريّ والمكتسب؛ فيكونُ كُلَّ مِنَ الضَّروريّ والمكتَسَبِ مُقسَّماً، والمفروضُ خِلافُهُ ؛ وهو أنَّ المقسَّم هو التَّصوُّرُ والتَّصديقُ ؛ لا أنَّهما قِسمَانِ، وأن يقرأ بالبناء للمفعول، والضَّرورة والاكتساب منصوبان على نزع الخافِضِ، وفيهما مِنَ التَّأويلِ مَا قُلنَا، وما أورد عليه بأنَّ النَّصبَ على نزع الخافض مقصورٌ على السَّماعِ ؛ يُجاب عنه بأنَّهُ كَثُرَ في كلامِهِم حَتَّى عُدَّ مِنَ المسامحات، وهَذان الاحتمالانِ مبنيَّانِ على ما ذكرَهُ في الأساس مِن أَنَّ قسمه واقتسمه بمعنى،
(1) (قولُهُ: نسختان) كذا بالنسخة التي بأيدينا ، والصَّوابُ نُسختَيْنِ بِالنَّصبِ؛ لأنَّه اسم «إِنَّ» مؤخراً عن خبرها الظَّرف . ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.

أي : التَصوُّرُ والتَّصديقُ (بِالضَّرُورَةِ)؛
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : بِالضَّرُورَةِ . . . إِلَخ الباءُ لِلملابسة؛ أي: انقساماً ملتبساً بالضرورة، ثمَّ يحتمل أن يكون المراد بالضَّرورة البداهة، وأن يكون المراد بها التَّطْعَ، والظَّاهِرُ الأول، وحينئذ؛ فقولُ الشَّارح : وإِنَّما كان تقسيم ... إلخ، من باب التنبيه لا من
العطار
وما في التسهيل من أنَّ افتعل يكون بمعنى فعل، وفسَّرَ الدَّوَّاني الاقتسام بالأخذ؛ حيث قال : أي يأخذُ كلٌّ مِنَ التَّصورِ والتَّصديقِ قِسماً من الضرورة والاكتساب؛ أي : الضَّروريّ والمكتسب .ا.هـ. فالضَّرورة والاكتساب مفعولان، قال العصام : وهوَ نِعْمَ التَّوجيه لو ساعده اللُّغة، ولم نجد في كتبِ اللُّغةِ أنَّ الافتعال يجي للاتِّخاذ، وقد يوجه بأنَّ المرادَ : يَقتسمان بينهما، ويلزمه أنْ يأخذ كلَّ مِنْهُمَا قِسماً ؛ انتهى. وأُجيب بثبوتِ مجيء الافتعال بمعنى الأخذ؛ نحو: ارتفق زيداً: أي: اتَّخَذَهُ رفيقاً، وهناك احتمال ثالث مبني على مجيء اقتسم بمعنى تقاسم؛ فقد ذكر التّسهيل مِن معاني افتعل تفاعل؛ فيقرأ يقتسمان بالبناء للفاعل، والضَّرورة والنَّظرُ على ظَاهرِهما مِن غير تأويل؛ مفعولان ليقتسمان، والمعنى حينئذ يأخذُ التَّصوُّرُ قِسماً من الضَّرورةِ؛ فيتحقَّقُ قسم ضروري، وقسماً مِنَ الاكتساب؛ فيتحقَّقُ تَصوُّرٌ مُكتسب، ويُقالُ مثله في التصديق ؛ فهو على حد قول الشاعر :
في كما صنع إنَّا اقتَسَمْنَا خُطَّتَيْنَا بِينَنَا فَحَمَلْتُ بَرَّةً واحتمَلتَ فَجار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : التَّصَوُّرُ والتَّصْدِيقُ) تقديمُ التَّصوُّرِ هنا على التصديق دون العكس المصنِّفُ؛ لأنَّ المنظور إليهِ هَهُنا ماصدقهما وأفرادهما، فإنَّهُ الَّذي يوصفُ بالضَّرورةِ والكَسْبِ ، وأمَّا مفهومُهُما ؛ فهو نظري كما سيأتي، قالَ شَارِحُ سُلَّم العلوم: ليس بين الضَّروري والكسبي تقابلُ الإيجابِ والسَّلبِ؛ لأنَّ المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسَّلبِ لا يخلو موضوع ما عنهُما ، وإلا ؛ لزم ارتفاعُ النَّقيضين، والموجوداتُ الغيبيَّةُ كُلُّها خالية عنهما، ولا التّضايفُ، وهو ظاهر، بل إنَّما يتصوَّرُ التّضادُّ أو العدمُ والمَلَكةُ، ولا بُدَّ في التَّضادّ من إمكان تعاقبهما على موضوع
Introduction of the author: (And they are divided) 
Dasuqi: (His statement: And they are divided ... etc.) is a start of what is a prelude for the need, and the need is the preservation of the mind from error in thinking referred to by his statement: 'So there is need for a rule ... etc.'

Then it is possible that by 'darura' is meant self-evidence, and that by it is meant certainty, and the apparent is the first. Then his statement: 'And indeed the division was ... etc.' is of the type of alerting, not of ... 
Attar: And what is in al-Tashil that 'ifta'ala' can have the meaning of 'fa'ala', and al-Dawani interpreted 'iqtisam' as taking; where he said: i.e., each of conception and assent takes a portion of necessity and acquisition; i.e., the necessary and the acquired. End. So 'al-darura' and 'al-iktisab' are objects. al-Isam said: It is an excellent interpretation if language supported it, and we do not find in books of language that 'ifti'al' comes for taking. It may be interpreted that the meaning is they divide between themselves, and it follows that each of them takes a portion; end. It was answered by the proof that 'ifti'al' comes with the meaning of taking; like 'irtadaqa Zaydan' i.e., made him a companion. There is a third possibility based on 'iqtasama' coming with the meaning of 'taqasama'; for al-Tashil mentions among the meanings of 'ifta'ala' is 'tafa'ala'; so it is read 'yaqtasimani' as active voice, and 'al-darura' and 'al-nazar' are on their apparent meaning without interpretation, as objects of 'yaqtasimani', and the meaning then is that conception takes a portion of necessity, so a necessary division is realized, and a portion of acquisition, so an acquired conception is realized, and the same is said concerning assent; it is like the poet's saying: 
'Indeed we divided our two lines between us, so I carried the good and you carried the wicked.' 
(His statement: i.e., conception and assent) Here he gives precedence to conception over assent, unlike the author's reverse, because what is looked at here is their referents and individuals, for they are described by necessity and acquisition. As for their concept, it is theoretical as will come. The commentator on Sullam al-Ulum said: There is not between the necessary and the acquired an opposition of affirmation and negation; because the two opposites by affirmation and negation do not have a subject devoid of both, otherwise the removal of the two contradictories would be necessary, and all unseen existences are devoid of them. Nor is it correlativity, which is apparent. Rather only contrariety or privation and possession can be imagined, and in contrariety there must be the possibility of them alternating upon a subject.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's statement 'And they are divided' (wa yanqasimani), referring to tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). The commentator, using the works of Dasuqi and Attar, explains the division of these two into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). The passage begins by identifying the text as the author's introduction, and then discusses the textual variants in the wording. There are two manuscript readings: one with the passive verb 'yanqasimani' (from infi'al) including the preposition 'ila', and another with the active verb 'yaqtasimani' (from ifti'al) without the preposition. The commentator analyzes each reading, noting the need for interpretation. The first reading, 'yanqasimani bi-d-darura', is taken as 'they are divided by necessity', where 'darura' means the necessary. The second reading, 'yaqtasimani', means 'they take portions', and the commentator objects that this would imply dividing necessary and theoretical into conception and assent, which reverses the intended division. The discussion includes grammatical points, such as the accusative case by removal of a preposition, and references to linguistic works like al-Asas and al-Tashil. A third possibility is that 'yaqtasimani' has the meaning of mutual division, as in the poet's verse. The passage then explains the meaning of 'bi-d-darura', noting that the preposition 'ba' indicates accompaniment, and 'darura' could mean self-evidence or certainty, with the first being more apparent. Finally, the passage addresses the order of mentioning conception before assent, and quotes a commentator on Sullam al-Ulum to clarify that the relationship between necessary and acquired is not that of contradictory opposites, but rather of contrariety or privation and possession. Throughout, the commentator emphasizes the precise wording and its implications for understanding the epistemological division. The passage is a detailed grammatical and logical analysis, typical of a sharh that expands on a compact matn statement.

The Conditional Defense of Necessary Knowledge
Refutation of objections against the division of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument that prevents all knowledge from being theoretical and identify its reliance on the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents.

Core Idea
If all knowledge were acquired, the mind would be trapped in an impossible loop or infinite regress.

The division of knowledge into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi) is not merely a classification; it is a logical necessity. The author defends this division by showing that if all knowledge were acquired through thinking, we would face an impossible circularity or infinite regress. But this proof rests on subtle assumptions about how concepts and assents relate, and even on the nature of the soul. Let's unpack the logical structure step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Under Defence
Find the claim that knowledge divides into necessary and acquired. The proof given is: if all knowledge were theoretical (requiring acquisition), then circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, which is impossible.
The claim is that some knowledge must be self-evident (by necessity).
2
Extract the Conditional Proof
The core argument is a conditional: 'If all knowledge (concepts and assents) were theoretical, then acquiring any piece of knowledge would involve either a circle or an infinite chain.' The author asserts the impossibility of these consequences, so the antecedent is false.
This is a modus tollens: If P then Q; Not Q; Therefore not P.
3
Locate the Objection
The objector says: The chain of theoretical items could end in a self-evident (badihi) concept or assent. For example, all concepts could be theoretical but end in a self-evident assent. This would avoid circularity and regress.
The objection breaks the conditional by providing a possible escape.
4
Find the Reply
The reply depends on the impossibility of acquiring a concept from an assent and vice versa. If concepts cannot be derived from assents and assents cannot be derived from concepts, then the chain cannot cross types. So if the chain starts with a concept, it must end with a concept, etc. This forces the regress.
The proof assumes this cross-acquisition is impossible.
5
Examine the Deeper Critique
Al-Jalal notes that this impossibility is not proven. Moreover, the regress argument also assumes the soul is temporal (created), following Aristotle. If the soul is eternal (Plato), an infinite series of thoughts in finite time is not impossible.
The proof is not absolutely certain; it rests on philosophical premises.

Introduction of the author of the matn. i.e., according to necessity. Dasuqi: Chapter of the proof; because necessary things may be pointed out due to their hiddenness from some minds, so it is not objected that necessary things are not proven and the commentator has proven them; i.e., he established a proof for them, and on the second probability; so the commentator's statement: 'because they are... etc.' is from the chapter of demonstration, and this is unlike the necessity which is one of the divisions of knowledge, for what is meant by it is self-evidence only. (His statement: i.e., according to necessity) i.e., in a way, and its 'ba' is for accompaniment, and its addition to necessity is for clarification. Al-Attar. One, so everything that is characterized by one of the two opposites; it is possible for it to be characterized by the other, and in the case of the opposites of privation and possession, it is necessary that the subject of privation be possible of possession, and on both estimates; so it is necessary that the necessary be possible of being acquired, and it is evident that the presential is impossible to be characterized by acquiredness, and similarly the eternal from knowledge; for if it could be acquired; it could be obtained after intellectual motion, so it would not be eternal. Therefore; the classification must be restricted to the temporal concept and assent. (His statement: i.e., according to necessity) He repelled by it the illusion that the 'ba' is causative, and it is not compatible with its requirement that the cause of the division is necessity, and it is not so, but rather it is a description of it, then it is probable that what is meant by it is the aspect of the proposition, and that is apparent from the commentator's practice; by the evidence of his inference to that by his statement: 'And it was only... etc.', and it is probable that what is meant by it is self-evidence, so the inference is either a reminder, or on the claim that this division is necessary, and in al-Dawwani: what is meant by it is self-evidence, and its justification is that referring to self-evidence is safer than burdening the inference upon it with the argument: if all of everything were theoretical; circularity or infinite regress would follow, or if all were self-evident we would not need thinking in any of them, for it, with what it contains of dependency on the impossibility of acquiring assent from concept then on the temporality of the soul as is well-known; is not completed except by claiming self-evidence in the proof and its parts, and that is sufficient to negate the acquiredness of all; so there is no need for the proof upon it, then there must be a claim of self-evidence in the establishment of the need for thinking, and that itself is the claim of self-evidence in the non-self-evidence of all. Dasuqi: (His statement: to necessity) i.e., to the possessor of necessity and the possessor of acquisition; because the division is only for the one described by necessity and the one described by acquisition, not for necessity and acquisition themselves, or he uttered necessity and meant the necessary, and uttered acquisition and meant... al-Attar: So it appears that the inference ultimately reduces to a claim of self-evidence in the desired; so let one be satisfied with it from the beginning. End of his speech. And its clarification is that he inferred in the original this claim; which is our statement: (And they are divided... etc.) by the mentioned proof, so he objected to the proof with the permissibility that all concepts are theoretical, and the chain of acquisition ends with a self-evident assent; so no circularity or infinite regress follows, and he also permitted that all assents are theoretical, and the chain of acquisition ends with a self-evident concept; so no circularity; no infinite regress either, and they replied that the demonstration depends on the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents and vice versa, so if it is established; the discussion is established, otherwise; not, and al-Jalal said in the gloss of al-Qutb: that the proof of the impossibility of acquiring concept from assent and vice versa is not established, but he said: the proof is established on the assumption of the absence of acquiring concept from assent and vice versa; whether it is impossible or not, for on the assumption of its absence, the obtaining of concepts or assents would be by way of circularity or infinite regress definitely. End. And also this is not an infinite regress; rather it is the summoning of an infinite number of things in a finite time; which is the time of acquisition, and the impossibility of that is built upon the view of the temporality of the soul, which is the doctrine of Aristotle and those who followed him. As for the view of Plato who says it is eternal; what was mentioned is not established, for why is it not permissible that infinite demands be acquired in infinite times? And also on the assumption that all concepts and assents are theoretical; our statement: if all were theoretical; circularity or infinite regress would follow, would be a theoretical assent, and each of the concepts mentioned in it would be theoretical, and also your statement: the consequent is false, so the antecedent is similar, would be a theoretical assent, and the concepts mentioned in it are also theoretical, so in acquiring these concepts and assents, there would be need for acquisition, and the impossible circularity or infinite regress would follow, so the inference would be...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a previous commentary on the division of knowledge into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). The super-commentator (likely al-Dasuqi) addresses the meaning of 'bi hasab al-darura' (according to necessity) and the proof that all knowledge cannot be solely theoretical. The passage begins by explaining that necessary truths may need pointing out due to their subtlety, and the commentator's proof is valid. It then discusses the classification of knowledge into concept and assent, limiting it to temporal instances because necessary knowledge cannot be acquired, nor can eternal knowledge. The core argument against the objection that all knowledge could be theoretical is presented: if all concepts were theoretical, the chain would end in a self-evident assent, and similarly for assents. The reply requires the impossibility of acquiring concepts from assents and vice versa. Al-Jalal notes that this impossibility is not proven, but the argument works on the assumption of its absence. Finally, the passage critiques the proof's reliance on the soul's temporality (Aristotle's view) and notes that on Plato's view of an eternal soul, the argument fails. The lesson demonstrates the epistemological foundations of the division and the subtle debates around it.

The division of knowledge into necessary and acquired is defended by a conditional argument that if all knowledge were acquired, circularity or infinite regress would result, but this argument relies on assumptions about cross-acquisition and the nature of the soul.

Since circularity and infinite regress are impossible, some rulings must be known by necessity (darura).' Write out the modus tollens form explicitly.

Al-Attar's Critique of the Commentator on the Definition of 'Alam
Al-Attar discusses the linguistic and logical status of 'alam and critiques a previous commentator's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in the commentator's understanding of ‘ālam and evaluate al-‘Aṭṭār’s critique using the concept of qadr mushtarak.

Core Idea
‘Ālam is a common meaning (qadr mushtarak) for all genera, not a homonym or a collective noun.

In this passage, al-‘Aṭṭār refines the definition of ‘ālam and exposes a commentator’s error in assuming that its individuals are particular beings whose origination is known intuitively. By clarifying that ‘ālam denotes a single meaning shared by all genera (mā siwā Allāh), he shows that the assent ‘the world is originated’ remains theoretical even when applied to every genus. This lesson walks through his argument and its logical implications for theological epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator's claim
The commentator claimed that the assent 'the world is originated' is only correct if meant as a universal affirmative (every individual of mā siwā Allāh), and that knowledge of origination of some individuals is intuitive. Find this claim in the passage.
The commentator is referred to as al-muḥashshī (the glossator).
2
Understand al-‘Aṭṭār’s definition of ‘ālam
Al-‘Aṭṭār defines ‘ālam as a name for the common meaning among all genera: being other than God. It is not a homonym like ‘ayn, nor a collective noun. It applies to each genus and to all genera, like 'human' applies to individuals.
The term qadr mushtarak is key: a single shared meaning.
3
Recognize the logical mistake
The commentator treats the individuals of ‘ālam as particular entities (e.g., a specific stone or person). Al-‘Aṭṭār shows they are genera (e.g., bodies, spirits). Thus knowledge of their origination is not intuitive but theoretical.
The difference between individuals as particulars vs. as genera is crucial.
4
Assess the critique's validity
Evaluate whether al-‘Aṭṭār’s reasoning holds. Does it correctly apply the distinction between a common meaning and its instances? Is the commentator's error clearly exposed?
Al-‘Aṭṭār's critique aligns with standard kalām usage and logical principles.

العطار فَذَكَرَهُ تصريحاً بِمَا عَلِمَ ضِمْنَاً أو حَمْلاً للاكتساب على المعنى اللُّغويّ، وَهُوَ مُطلَقُ التحصيل ; لكنَّهُ لا يُلائِمُ الإيجاز المطلوب في هذهِ الرّسالة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَكَالتَّصْدِيقِ بِأَنَّ الْعَالَمَ حَادِثٌ في حاشية عبد الحكيم على الخيالي : العَالمُ اسمٌ مَوضوع للقدر المشترك بينَ جميعِ الأجناس؛ أعني : كونَهُ مَا سِوَى الله، فإنَّ القَولَ بِتعدُّدِ الوضعِ بِحسبِ كُلِّ جنسٍ كَلَفْظِ العَينِ؛ قول بِلا دليل، وكَذَا مُجُعِلَ الوضع عاما ; والموضوعُ لَهُ خاصا ، فإِنَّهُ مَخصُوص بمواضع عديدة، وإذا كانَ مَوضُوعاً لمعنى واحدٍ مُشتَرَكِ بينَ جميع الأجناس; يجوزُ إطلاق العام على كُلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأجناس، وعلى كُلِّهَا إطلاقُ الكُلّيّ على جزئياتِهِ; كَإطلاقِ الإنسانِ على كُلِّ مِنْ زيد وعمرو وعليّ وعليّ كُلّها، وليسَ اسماً للمجموع، وإلا ; لَمَا صَعَّ جَمعُهُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى : رَبِّ الْعَالَمِينَ ﴾ [الفاتحة: ٢]، والقولُ بالاشتراكِ بينَ الكُلِّ وكُلِّ واحدٍ خِلَاف الأصل لا يُصارُ إليهِ بِلا ضرورة داعية ا.هـ. , فَعُلِمَ أنَّ أفراد ذلكَ القدرِ المشتركِ أجناس لا أشخاص; فَمَا قالَهُ المحشّي إِنَّ تمثيل التصديقِ النَّظريّ بِقولِنَا : العَالَمُ حادِثٌ إِنَّما يصح إذا أُريد الموجبة الكُلِّيَّة، أعني : كُلّ فردٍ مِن أفرادِ مَا سِوَى اللهِ تعالى نظري، إِذْ لَا شَكٍّ أَنَّ العِلْمَ بِحدوثِ بعض الأفرادِ ضَروري .ا.هـ. غيرُ مُستقيم; لأنَّ أفرادَ القَدْرِ المشتركِ الموضوعِ لَهُ
Al-‘Aṭṭār: So he mentioned it explicitly for what he knew implicitly, or taking acquisition in the linguistic sense, which is absolute attainment; but it does not suit the brevity required in this epistle. His statement: 'And like the assent that the world is originated' — in the gloss of ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm on al-Khayālī: ‘al-‘ālam is a name placed for the common meaning among all genera; namely, that it is everything other than God. For the claim of multiple word-uses according to each genus, like the word ‘ayn, is a claim without proof. Likewise, making the word-use general and the referent particular is a specific thing in many places. And if it is placed for a single meaning common among all genera, it is permissible to apply the general term to every one of the genera, and to all of them as the application of a universal to its particulars, like the application of 'human' to every one of Zayd, ‘Amr, and ‘Alī, and to all of them. It is not a name for the collection; otherwise, its plural would not be sound, as in the words of the Exalted: 'Lord of the worlds' (al-Fātiḥah: 2). The claim of sharing between the whole and each individual is contrary to the principle, and one should not resort to it without a compelling necessity.' End quote. It is thus known that the individuals of that common meaning are genera, not individuals. So what the commentator said, that the exemplification of theoretical assent by our statement 'the world is originated' is only correct if the universal affirmative is intended, meaning: every individual of what is other than God is theoretical, since there is no doubt that knowledge of the origination of some individuals is necessary, is unsound, because the individuals of the common meaning to which it is applied...
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage from al-‘Aṭṭār provides a detailed critique of a previous commentator's view on the term ‘ālam (world) as used in the example of theoretical assent. Al-‘Aṭṭār begins by noting that the matn author mentioned the example explicitly to clarify what was implicit, or perhaps he took 'acquisition' in its linguistic sense (absolute attainment), but this does not suit the conciseness of the epistle. He then quotes from the gloss of ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm on al-Khayālī, who argues that ‘ālam is a name for the common meaning among all genera: everything other than God. The gloss refutes the idea that ‘ālam has multiple word-uses for each genus (like the word ‘ayn meaning eye, spring, etc.) as baseless. It also rejects the notion that the word-use is general while the referent is specific, because that would apply in many places. Instead, if ‘ālam is a single common meaning, it can be applied to each genus individually and to all collectively, just like the universal 'human' applies to each individual and to all humanity. It is not a name for the collection; otherwise, its plural 'worlds' (al-‘ālamīn) would not be correct. The claim of homonymy (shared meaning) is against the norm. From this, al-‘Aṭṭār concludes that the individuals of that common meaning are genera (like the genus of bodies, the genus of spirits), not individuals (like a particular stone). Therefore, what the commentator (al-muḥashshī) said — that the assertion 'the world is originated' is only valid if it is a universal affirmative meaning every individual without exception, and that knowledge of the origination of some individuals is intuitive — is unsound. Because the individuals of the common meaning are genera, not particular individuals, so the universal judgment about them is still theoretical.

Key Terms
عالم
قدر مشترك
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-‘Aṭṭār's discussion, why is it invalid to say that knowledge of the origination of some individuals of the world is intuitive?
A) Because all individuals of the world are intuitive
B) Because the individuals of the common meaning 'ālam are genera, not specific individuals
C) Because the commentator is always correct
D) Because the world is not originated

Key Takeaway
Al-‘Aṭṭār establishes that ‘ālam denotes a single common meaning (mā siwā Allāh) whose individuals are genera, not particulars, so the assent of its origination is universal and theoretical, not rooted in intuitive knowledge of specific beings.

What is Naẓar?
Dassuqi defines naẑar and distinguishes it from acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define naẓar in its technical sense and distinguish it from iktisāb.

Core Idea
Naẓar is the process, not the product — the tool, not the treasure.

Dassuqi's gloss refines the definition of naẓar, correcting a previous commentator who conflated the reasoning process with its result. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the epistemological framework of Islamic logic: some knowledge is acquired through effort, and naẓar is that effort.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic text: 'النَّظر' (naẓar). Note that Dassuqi explicitly distinguishes it from 'الاكتساب' (iktisāb).
The pronoun 'هو' in the passage refers to naẑar.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
Dassuqi defines naẓar as 'الملاحظة' (observation/mulāḥaẓa), which is 'توجُهُ النَّفْسِ والتفاتُها إلى المعقول' (the soul's direction and attention to the intelligible). This is the broader category.
Mulāḥaẓa is synonymous with naẑar in this context.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is the purpose: 'لأجل التحصيل' (for the sake of acquisition). Naẓar is not any observation, but observation aimed at acquiring knowledge.
This distinguishes naẑar from passive perception.
4
Check completeness of the definition
Does this definition include all instances of naẓar and exclude everything else? Dassuqi emphasizes that the purpose clause is essential; without it, the definition would be too broad. The definition also excludes iktisāb itself, which is the product, not the process.
The definition is complete and precise according to the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن أمَّا الملازَمَةُ ؛ فظاهرة. وأمَّا بطلان القسم الأوَّلِ من التَّالي ؛ فَلِاحْتِياجنا في بعض التَّصوراتِ وبعض التصديقات إلى كشبٍ ونَظَرٍ كما مرَّ . وأمَّا بطلان القسم الثاني منه، فَلِبَداهَةِ بَعضِ التّصوراتِ، وبعض التصديقات على ما مرّ . (وَهُوَ)؛ أي : الاكتسابُ بالنَّظَرِ الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : أَمَّا الْمُلَازَمَةُ) أي : بين المقدَّم والتّالي فظاهرة)؛ أي: لأنَّه لا واسطة. (قَوْلُهُ : الْقِسْمِ الْأَوَّلِ وهو كونُ الجميعِ بديهيا، وقوله: (القسم الثاني)؛ وهو كون الجميع كسبيا . (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا مَرَّ) أي : في قولِهِ : كَتصورِ العقل والإنسان، وكالتصديق بأنَّ العالم . . . إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : فَلِبَدَاهَةِ بَعْضِ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ وَبعض التَّصْدِيقَاتِ كَمَا مَرَّ) أي في قولِهِ : كتصورِ الحرارةِ والبرودة، وكالتصديق بأنَّ النَّفى والإثبات لا يجتمعان وَلَا يرتفعان)، وهذا الدَّليلُ الَّذي ذكرَهُ الشَّارِحُ يُسمَّى بدليل الخلف، وهو إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه، فالمطلوب انقسامُهمَا لِلقسمين ، ونقيضُهُ عدم الانقسام، وَلَا شَكٍّ أَنَّهُ هو الَّذِي أبطله (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ) أي : النَّظرُ لا الاكتساب خلافاً للشَّارح ؛ إذ المراد بالاكتساب فيما سبق المكتسب، وهو ليسَ نَفْسَ الملاحظة؛ إذ الملاحظة: توجُهُ النَّفْسِ والتفاتُها إلى المعقول ؛ أي إلى ما حصلَتْ صورته في العقل لتحصيل ؛ أي: لأجل تحصيل ... إلخ، حصل بالفعل أم لا، وإنَّما قُيّد بذلك؛ لأنَّ النَّظر ليسَ إِلَّا الملاحظة ؛ لأجل التحصيل . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : أَي الِاكْتِسَابُ ... إلخ) في عودةِ الضَّمير على ذلكَ تَخلُّصٌ مِمَّا يلزمُ عليهِ من ارتكاب التَّجوز ؛ بناءً على أنَّ النَّظر حقيقةً ؛ هو حركةُ النَّفْسِ في المعقولات؛ :أي: ارتسامُها فيها بالاستقراض من المتصرّفة، ولا شَكٍّ أنَّ النَّفْسَ تُلاحظها عند
The introduction of the author: As for the entailment, it is apparent. As for the falsity of the first division of the consequent, it is because of our need in some concepts and some assents to acquisition and consideration as previously. As for the falsity of the second division, it is because of the intuitiveness of some concepts and some assents as previously. (And it) i.e. acquisition by consideration. Dassuqi: His saying 'As for the entailment' i.e. between the antecedent and the consequent is apparent, i.e. because there is no intermediary. His saying 'the first division' which is that all are intuitive, and his saying 'the second division' which is that all are acquired. His saying 'as previously' i.e. in his saying: like the concept of intellect and human, and the assent that the world... etc. His saying 'it is because of the intuitiveness of some concepts and some assents as previously' i.e. in his saying: like the concept of heat and cold, and the assent that negation and affirmation cannot coexist and cannot both be absent. This proof that the commentator mentioned is called proof by contradiction, which is establishing the desired by invalidating its opposite. So the desired is their division into the two sections, and its opposite is non-division, and there is no doubt that it is what he invalidated. His saying (and it) i.e. consideration, not acquisition, contrary to the commentator; because what is meant by acquisition previously is the acquired, and it is not the same as observation; for observation is the soul's direction and attention to the intelligible i.e. to what its form has been obtained in the mind for the purpose of acquisition i.e. for the sake of acquiring... etc., whether it has been obtained actually or not. It is only restricted because consideration is nothing but observation for the sake of acquisition. Al-Attar: His saying 'i.e. acquisition... etc.' in the return of the pronoun to that is a way out of what would necessitate committing metaphor, based on the fact that consideration in reality is the soul's movement among intelligibles, i.e. its impression in them by borrowing from the active intellect, and there is no doubt that the soul observes them when...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the matn (author's introduction) and a gloss defining naẓar (consideration). The matn explains the proof by contradiction in detail: the entailment between antecedent and consequent is immediate; the falsity of the first division (all intuitive) is because some knowledge requires acquisition; the falsity of the second division (all acquired) is because some knowledge is intuitive. This proof is identified as dalīl al-khalf. Then Dassuqi defines naẓar: it is the soul's direction (tawajjuh) and attention (iltifāt) to intelligibles for the purpose of acquisition (taḥṣīl). He distinguishes it from acquisition itself (iktisāb), correcting the commentator (shāriḥ) who identified naẓar with acquisition. Dassuqi insists that naẓar is the process, not the product. Al-Attar adds that naẓar is the soul's movement (ḥaraka) in intelligibles, borrowing from the active intellect. The example from the matn: concepts like 'intellect' are acquired, 'heat' is intuitive; assents like 'the world is created' are acquired, while the law of non-contradiction is intuitive. This clarifies the epistemological basis: some knowledge is self-evident, some requires effort, and naẓar is the tool for the latter.

Key Terms
نظر
اكتساب
ملاحظة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Dassuqi, what is the correct definition of naẓar?
A) The acquired knowledge that results from reasoning.
B) The soul's observation of intelligibles for the purpose of acquisition.
C) Intuitive knowledge that requires no effort.
D) The active intellect's influence on the soul.

Key Takeaway
Naẓar is the soul's purposeful observation of intelligibles to acquire knowledge, distinct from the acquisition itself.

Proof by Contradiction: Dalīl al-Khalf
Commentator identifies the proof as dalīl al-khalf and explains its structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction as used in the passage and apply it to an Islamic theological argument.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction establishes truth by showing that its opposite leads to absurdity.

The commentator identifies the logical structure behind the author's argument about the division of knowledge into intuitive and acquired. This structure is dalīl al-khalf, a powerful tool in Islamic logic and theology. Understanding it allows you to prove any proposition by eliminating its negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb)
The passage states: 'the desired is their division into the two sections'. This is the claim that knowledge (both concepts and assents) is divided into intuitive and acquired.
The 'two sections' refer to all intuitive or all acquired, but the correct division is that some are intuitive and some acquired.
2
State the opposite (naqīḍ)
The opposite of the desired is 'non-division', i.e., either all knowledge is intuitive or all is acquired. The proof aims to invalidate these two possibilities.
The opposite is a disjunction: either all intuitive or all acquired.
3
Show the absurdity of the first alternative
The first alternative (all intuitive) is false because 'we need in some concepts and assents to acquisition and consideration'. So it leads to a contradiction with observed reality.
Example: concept of 'intellect' is acquired, not intuitive.
4
Show the absurdity of the second alternative
The second alternative (all acquired) is false because some concepts and assents are intuitive, e.g., the law of non-contradiction.
Thus both alternatives are invalidated.
5
Conclude the original desired
Since both alternatives of the opposite are impossible, the original proposition (that knowledge is divided into both intuitive and acquired) is established.
This is proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Attar adds that naẓar is the soul's movement (ḥaraka) in intelligibles, borrowing from the active intellect. The example from the matn: concepts like 'intellect' are acquired, 'heat' is intuitive; assents like 'the world is created' are acquired, while the law of non-contradiction is intuitive. This clarifies the epistemological basis: some knowledge is self-evident, some requires effort, and naẓar is the tool for the latter.

Key Terms
دليل الخلف
ملازمة
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the name of the proof structure described in the passage when the commentator says 'This proof... is called...'?
A) Qiyas iqtirani
B) Dalīl al-khalf
C) Dalī al-naẓar
D) Burhan al-siddiqin

Key Takeaway
Proof by contradiction (dalīl al-khalf) establishes a claim by showing that its opposite leads to an absurdity, making it a fundamental tool in Islamic logic.

The Imperative of Prior Knowledge in Seeking the Unknown
Author responds to an objection that seeking the unknown is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the sought object in nazar must be partly known, and identify the two logical pitfalls it avoids.

Core Idea
Seeking the unknown requires a known starting point, not absolute ignorance.

This passage presents a common objection to the very possibility of systematic inquiry (nazar): how can you seek something you don't know? The author's answer clarifies the nature of the sought object—it must be partly known and partly unknown. This principle is foundational for the theory of knowledge acquisition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (naqd)
The objection claims that if the sought object is unknown, the soul would be seeking the absolutely unknown, which is impossible.
2
Identify the answer (jawab)
The answer affirms that the sought object cannot be unknown in all respects; it must be known in one respect (e.g., genus) and unknown in another (e.g., differentia).
3
Understand the two logical pitfalls avoided
This answer avoids two absurdities: seeking the absolutely unknown (impossible) and obtaining what is already possessed (tahsil al-hasil).
4
Apply to your own study of logic
When learning a new definition or argument, identify what you already know (e.g., the genus) and what you need to discover (the differentia).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنْ قُلْتَ: إذا كان المطلوب مجهولاً ؛ يلزم أن تكون النفس طالبة للمجهول المطلق، وهو مُحالٌ ، والجواب : أنَّه قد تحقَّقَ أَنَّهُ لا بُدَّ أنْ لَا يكون المطلوب مجهولاً مِنْ كلّ الوجوه، بلْ لا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ معلوماً بوجه؛ لئلا يلزم طلب المجهول المطلق، ومجهولاً بوجه آخر ؛ لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل.
If you say: When the sought object is unknown, it would necessitate that the soul seeks the absolutely unknown, which is impossible. And the answer: It has been established that it is necessary that the sought object is not unknown in all respects, but rather it must be known in one respect to avoid seeking the absolutely unknown, and unknown in another respect to avoid obtaining what is already obtained.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange typical of a hashiya. The author presents an objection (naqd) and its answer (jawab). The objection states that if the sought object (al-matlub) in the process of thought (nazar) is absolutely unknown, then the soul would be seeking something of which it has no awareness at all, which is impossible. The answer refines this: the sought object cannot be unknown from every perspective; it must be known in one aspect (e.g., its genus or some attribute) and unknown in another aspect (e.g., its specific differentia or conclusion). This avoids both the impossible seeking of the absolutely unknown and the redundancy of seeking what is already fully known (tahsil al-hasil). This principle is foundational for the theory of acquisition (iktisab) of knowledge: every new knowledge requires some prior known as a starting point.

Key Terms
المجهول المطلق
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't the sought object be absolutely unknown?
A) Because that would require seeking what already exists.
B) Because the soul would be seeking something with no prior connection, which is impossible.
C) Because every sought object is known by definition.
D) Because seeking is limited to material objects.

Key Takeaway
Every inquiry into the unknown must start from something already known; otherwise inquiry is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take any definition from the five universals (e.g., 'man is a rational animal'). Identify what aspect is known beforehand (genus: animal) and what is unknown (differentia: rational). Explain how this matches the author's principle.
?
In a qiyas (syllogism), the middle term is often known from the premises while the conclusion is unknown. Use the principle of this lesson to explain why the conclusion is not absolutely unknown.

Why 'al-Ma'qūl' Instead of 'al-Ma'lūm'
Commentator's clarification of the term 'al-ma'qūl'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the inclusive scope of 'al-ma'qūl' and identify what it covers that 'al-ma'lūm' does not.

Core Idea
'Ma'qūl' includes the conjectured and the compoundly unknown, not just the known.

The commentator begins by clarifying why the author chose the term 'al-ma'qūl' over more common terms like 'al-ma'lūm'. This choice is not arbitrary; it signals the full range of objects that thought can consider, including what is conjectured or even unknown in a compound way. Understanding this scope is the first step in grasping the epistemological framework of the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms compared
Find 'al-ma'qūl' and 'al-ma'lūm' in the Arabic text. Note that the commentator explicitly contrasts them.
2
List the categories included
According to the text, 'al-ma'qūl' includes what is known (ma'lūm), conjectured (maznūn), and unknown with compound ignorance (majhūl jahlan murakkaban). Also, it includes concepts (taṣawwur) and assents (taṣdīq), whether simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab).
Compound ignorance means one does not know and is unaware of one's ignorance.
3
Determine what is excluded
By implication, 'al-ma'lūm' excludes what is conjectured or compoundly unknown. It only covers what is actually known.
4
Connect to the definition
Why is this inclusive scope important for the definition of 'mulāhazat al-ma'qūl'? It ensures the definition covers all possible objects of thought, not just certain knowledge, which is essential for a complete account of reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُهُ : الْمَعْقُوْلِ) عبر به دونَ المعلوم؛ ليشمل ما كانَ معلوماً أو مظنوناً أو مجهولاً جهلاً مركباً، وسواء كانَ المعقول تصوراً أو تصديقاً ، مُفرداً أو مركباً . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : مُلَاحَظَةُ الْمَعْقُوْلِ تحريرُ المقامِ أنَّهُ لا شُبهة فِي أَنَّ كُلَّ مَجهولٍ لا يُمكِنُ اكتسابُهُ مِن أيِّ مَعلوم اتَّفق ؛ بل لا بُدَّ مِن معلوماتٍ مُناسبة له، ومَعلومٌ أَنَّهُ لا يُمكِنُ تحصيله من تلك المعلوماتِ على أي وجه كان؛ بل لا بُدَّ هناكَ مِن ترتيبٍ مُعيَّنٍ فيما بين تلك المعلومات، ومن هيئةٍ مخصوصة عارِضَةٍ لها بسبب ذلك الترتيب، فإذا حصلَ لَنَا شُعورٌ بأمرٍ تصوري أو تصديقي وحاولنا تحصيله على وجه أكمل؛ فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يتحرَّكَ الذَّهنُ في المعلومات المخزونة عندَهُ منتقلاً مِن مَعلوم إلى آخَرَ؛ حتّى يجد المعلوماتِ المناسبة لذلك المطلوب، وهِيَ المسمَّاةُ بِمباديه، ثمَّ لا بُدَّ أيضاً أن يتحرَّكَ في تلك المبادي بترتيبها ترتيباً خاصا يؤدّي إلى ذلكَ المطلوب، فَهُناكَ حركتانِ ؛ مبدأ الأولى منهما : هو المطلوب المشعورُ بِه بذلك الوجهِ النَّاقِصِ، ومُنتَهَاهَا : آخِرُ ما يحصلُ مِن تلك المبادي، ومبدأُ الثَّانيةِ أَوَّلُ مَا يوضَعُ مِنَ التَّرتيب ، ومُنتهاها المشعورُ بِهِ على الوجه الأكمل، فَحقيقةُ النَّظَرِ المتوسط بينَ المعلومِ والمجهول، وهو مجموع هاتين الحركتين اللَّتَينِ هُمَا مِن قبيل الحركة في الكيفيَّاتِ، فالمتقدِّمُونَ ذهبوا إلى أنَّ الفكر مجموع الحركتين، وذهب المتأخّرونَ إلى أنَّهُ التَّرتيبُ اللَّازِمُ لِلحركةِ الثَّانية، ويُرادِفُ الفكرُ النَّظر على القولين، قال أبو الفتح : ورُبَّما يُفرَّقُ بينَهُما بأنَّ الفِكْرَ مجموع الحركتين أو الترتيبُ اللَّازِمُ لَهُمَا والنَّظَرَ مُلاحظة المعقولات الواقعة في ضمن الحركتين أو الترتيب، ويدلُّ عليهِ قَولُ ناقد المحصل أنَّهما كالمترادِفَينِ ، قال : والظَّاهِرُ أنَّ تعريف المصنفِ مبني على هذا ا.هـ.
His saying: 'al-ma'qūl' – he expressed it with this term instead of 'al-ma'lūm' to include what is known, conjectured, or unknown with compound ignorance, whether the ma'qūl is a concept or an assent, simple or compound. Al-‘Aṭṭār. His saying: 'mulāḥaẓat al-ma'qūl' – clarification of the topic: there is no doubt that every unknown cannot be acquired from any known at random; rather, there must be suitable knowns. And it is known that it cannot be obtained from those knowns in any manner; rather, there must be a specific arrangement among those knowns and a particular form (hay'a) that accrues to them due to that arrangement. So when we become aware of something, conceptual or assertive, and we attempt to obtain it in a more complete manner, the mind must move among the stored information, passing from one known to another, until it finds the suitable pieces of information for that sought object, which are called its principles (mabādi'). Then it must also move among those principles by ordering them in a special way that leads to that sought object. So there are two movements: the starting point of the first is the sought object known in that deficient aspect, and its endpoint is the last of those principles obtained; the starting point of the second is the first item placed in the arrangement, and its endpoint is the sought object known in the more complete aspect. So the reality of naẓar is the intermediary between the known and the unknown, and it is the sum of these two movements, which are of the type of motion in qualities. The early scholars held that fikr is the sum of the two movements, and the later scholars held that it is the arrangement necessary for the second movement. And fikr is synonymous with naẓar according to both views. Abu al-Fatḥ said: Sometimes a distinction is made between them: that fikr is the sum of the two movements or the arrangement necessary for both, and naẓar is the observation of the intelligibles that occur within the two movements or the arrangement. This is supported by the statement of Nāqid al-Muḥaṣṣil that they are like synonyms. He said: It appears that the author's definition is based on this. End of quotation.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn definition of 'mulāḥaẓat al-ma'qūl' (observation of the intelligible). The commentator first explains why the author used 'ma'qūl' instead of 'ma'lūm': to include what is conjectured or unknown by compound ignorance, as well as concepts and assents, simple and compound. Then the commentator (al-‘Aṭṭār) clarifies the topic. He establishes that not any known can lead to any unknown; the knowns must be suitable. Furthermore, simply having suitable knowns is not enough—they must be arranged in a specific order with a particular form (hay'a) to yield the sought knowledge. The process begins when we have a deficient awareness of something and attempt to attain a more complete knowledge. This triggers two movements in the mind: first, a search among stored information to find appropriate principles (mabādi'), and second, an arrangement of those principles in a specific way to reach the sought knowledge. The definition of naẓar (speculative reasoning) is the mean between the known and the unknown, encompassing both movements. There is a historical difference: early scholars identified thought (fikr) with both movements, while later scholars considered it only the arrangement of the second movement. According to both views, fikr and naẓar are synonymous. Then Abū al-Fatḥ suggests a possible distinction: fikr is the sum of movements or arrangement, while naẓar is the observation of the intelligibles within that process. However, the author of the matn likely treated them as synonyms, as indicated by Nāqid al-Muḥaṣṣil.

Key Terms
مَعْقُول
مَعْلُوم
جَهْل مُرَكَّب
تَصَوُّر
تَصْدِيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, 'al-ma'qūl' includes all of the following EXCEPT:
A) what is known (ma'lūm)
B) what is conjectured (maznūn)
C) what is impossible (muḥāl)
D) what is unknown with compound ignorance (majhūl jahlan murakkaban)

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-ma'qūl' is deliberately chosen to encompass all objects of thought, including the conjectured and the compoundly unknown, ensuring the definition covers the entire process of reasoning.

?
Consider the statement 'The Quran is uncreated.' For a person who has not yet studied kalam, is this a ma'qūl or a ma'lūm? Explain using the text's definitions.
?

The Two Movements of Thought and the Role of Arrangement
Author's explanation of the two movements of thought and the definition of naẓar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the two movements of thought and explain why both are necessary for acquiring knowledge.

Core Idea
Thought is a two-stage process: find suitable principles, then arrange them in a specific order.

The commentator elaborates that merely having relevant knowns is not enough; they must be arranged in a particular way. This leads to the concept of two movements: a search for principles (mabādi') and a systematic arrangement (hay'a). This lesson unpacks these two phases and the definition of naẓar, along with historical views on fikr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two movements
Read the Arabic: 'فَهُناكَ حركتانِ'. The text explicitly states there are two movements. Locate the description of each.
2
Describe the first movement
The first movement starts from a deficient awareness of the sought object. The mind moves among stored information, passing from known to known, until it finds the suitable principles (mabādi'). Its endpoint is the last of those principles obtained.
3
Describe the second movement
The second movement begins with the first item placed in the arrangement. The mind moves through the principles in a specific order (hay'a) to reach the sought object in its more complete aspect.
4
Understand the role of arrangement
Why is a specific order necessary? Without it, the principles do not yield the sought knowledge. The form (hay'a) that arises from the arrangement is essential for the conclusion.
5
Grasp the definition of naẓar
Naẓar is defined as the sum of these two movements, mediating between the known and the unknown. The text also notes that early scholars identified fikr with both movements, later scholars only the second, but fikr and naẓar are synonymous on both views.

10 min
Why Nazar is Defined as 'Observation'
Author discusses three definitions of nazar, explains his choice, and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three definitions of nazar, explain why the author prefers 'observation', and evaluate the objection regarding coverage of single-term definitions.

Core Idea
The definition of nazar must cover both single-term and compound reflections; only 'observation' achieves this.

In this passage, the commentator discusses the three competing definitions of nazar (theoretical reflection) and explains why the author chose the definition of 'observation of intelligibles'. The key issue is whether the definition covers all types of nazar, especially those involving a single term. Let's walk through the reasoning behind this choice and the response to objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions of nazar
The passage states that nazar has three definitions. The one chosen is 'mulāhaza' (observation). The other two are not named explicitly but alluded to as not covering the single term (mufrad). One of them is the 'two movements' (harakatān) mentioned later.
The 'two movements' refer to the movement from premises to conclusion in a syllogism.
2
Understand why the other definitions fail to cover mufrad
The other two definitions cannot account for nazar that involves a single term (e.g., a single concept rather than a compound of premises). Supporters of those definitions tried to force coverage by saying mufrad occurs only through derivatives, which are compound in meaning, but the author argues this is insufficient.
Derivatives (mushtaqqat) contain both essence and attribute, making them compound, but a single-term definition is still a single term.
3
Analyse the objection to the chosen definition
Some object that the author's definition of observation is not absolute but limited to 'observation of intelligibles occurring within the two movements and arrangement', so it would not apply to single-term nazar either.
This objection claims the definition is implicitly restricted.
4
See the author's response
The author responds that in the section on kalam, nazar was defined as the two movements because single terms are not used there. But in logic, the rules must be general, so the definition must cover both single and compound nazar. He dismisses a gloss that tries to reconcile the two definitions as unnecessary.
This shows the principle that definitions in an art must be comprehensive.

And it is known from what we have mentioned previously that nazar has three definitions. The author chose this definition, which is 'observation' because the other two definitions do not cover definition by a single term. And they forced themselves in coverage by saying that it only occurs with derivatives, and derivatives are compound in terms of containing the essence and attribute, or in terms of being more general in concept, so there must be a specifying context. Therefore the definition by a compound consisting of the meaning of the derivative and the context, or that the lack of coverage does not harm because definition by a single term, as the Shaykh said, is rare and deficient, that is, little and deficient. So the definition of nazar by the mentioned definition is comprehensive, as al-Jalal said: all individuals of nazar without effort, whether it is by a single term or compound, known or conjectured, or unknown by compound ignorance. End quote. And the claim of its coverage of the single term was objected to by saying that observation is not absolute observation of the intelligible, but rather it is observation of the intelligibles occurring within the two movements and arrangement, so the author's definition does not apply to the single term either. Then the author defined nazar in the second section of this book which contains kalam as the two movements because nazar in the single term does not occur in the discussions of kalam, so there is no need to include it in the definition. But here, it is needed because the rules of the art must be general. And it is stupid the saying of some glosses: it is possible that what is here is a context indicating that he intended from the totality of the two movements there the observation mentioned here.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of the definition of nazar (theoretical reflection). The author reminds that nazar has three definitions. The author (al-Dasuqi) chose the definition as 'ملاحظة المعقولات' (observation of intelligibles) because the other two definitions fail to cover definition by a single term (ta'rif bil-mufrad). Those who adhere to the other definitions attempt to cover mufrad by claiming that it only occurs through derivatives (mushtaqqat), which are compound because they include essence and attribute, or because they are more general in concept, thus requiring a specifying context. Alternatively, they argue that lack of coverage is not harmful since definition by mufrad is rare and deficient. The author's definition is comprehensive, as al-Jalal al-Dawwani stated, covering all individuals of nazar whether single or compound, known or conjectured. Then an objection is presented: that 'observation' here is not absolute observation but specifically observation of intelligibles occurring within the two movements and arrangement, so it does not apply to the single term. The author responds by noting that in the second section of the book (on kalam), nazar was defined as the two movements because in kalam, single terms are not used, but here (in logic) the rules must be general, so the definition must cover single terms. The author dismisses a gloss that suggests the context here is a clue that the earlier definition of 'two movements' actually meant the same 'observation'.

The Definition of Nazar (Reflective Thinking)
Author defines nazar and explains its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of nazar (observation, two movements, ordering) and explain the purpose of acquiring the unknown.

Core Idea
Nazar is intentional, ordered observation aimed at acquiring the unknown.

In this passage, the author defines nazar as reflective thinking. The definition is precise: it requires intentional observation (ملاحظة) of intelligibles within two movements (حركتين) and ordering (ترتيب) for the purpose of acquiring the unknown (تحصيل المجهول). This definition distinguishes genuine reasoning from accidental or intuitive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four key terms
Find the words: observation (ملاحظة), two movements (حركتين), ordering (ترتيب), and acquisition of unknown (تحصيل المجهول).
Each term is essential for the definition.
2
Understand the role of observation
Observation must be intentional, not accidental. It is the mind's deliberate direction toward known intelligibles.
This excludes cases like sudden intuition.
3
Why two movements?
Reflective thinking involves a movement from known premises to unknown conclusions, typically via a middle term. Two movements refer to the logical progression.
Simple concepts without this movement are not nazar.
4
Purpose condition
The lam in 'li-tahsil' indicates purpose. The ordering must be for acquiring the unknown, not just for understanding the premise itself.
This excludes single-premise reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالتّعريف المذكور شامل، كَمَا قالَ الجلالُ : جميع أفراد النظرِ بلا كلفة؛ سواء كانَ بالمفرد أو المركَّب، معلوماً كان أو مظنوناً، أو مجهولاً بالجهل المركب ا.هـ.. ونُوقِش دعوى شُمولِهِ للمفرد بأنَّ الملاحظة ليسَتْ مُطلق ملاحظة المعقول ؛ بل هى ملاحظة المعقولات الواقعة في ضمن الحركتين والترتيب؛ فلا يصدق تعريف المصنفِ على المفرد أيضاً، ثمَّ إنَّ المصنِّفَ عَرَّفَ النَّظَرَ فِي القِسمِ الثَّانِي مِن هذا الكتابِ المُشتَمِل على عِلْم الكلام بالحركتين لما أنَّ النَّظَرَ في المفردِ لا يقعُ في مَبَاحِثِ عِلْمِ الكَلامِ ؛ فَلَا يحتاجُ لإدخاله في التَّعريف، وأمَّا هُنا ؛ فَمُحتاج إلى ذلك ؛ لأنَّ قَوَاعِدَ الفَنِّ يجب أن تكونَ عامةً، ومِنَ الباردِ قولُ بعض الحواشي : يحتمل أن يكون ما هنا قرينةً على أنَّه أرادَ مِن مجموع الحركتين هناك؛ الملاحظة المذكورةَ هُنَا . (قَوْلُهُ : لِتَحْصِيْلِ الْمَجْهُولِ اللَّامُ لِلأجل ؛ أي: الملاحظة التي يكونُ البَاعثُ عليها التحصيل ؛ فَخَرجَت المقدّمة الواحدة؛ لأنَّ التَّرتيب فيها ليسَ لِتحصيل المجهول ؛ بل لتحصيل المقدّمة، ودخل قياس المساواة والاستلزام بواسطة عَكْسِ النقيض، وإن أخرجُوهُما عن القياس لتقييدِهِم هناك الاستلزام؛ بأنْ يكونَ لِذاتِ القياس ولا لزوم فيهما بحسبِ الذَّاتِ، ودخل أيضاً النَّظَرُ فِي الدَّليل الثَّاني بعد الدليل الأوَّلِ؛ لأنَّ المقصودَ مِنهُ العِلْمُ بوجه دلالتِهِ وهو مجهولٌ، وَإِنَّمَا قالَ: لتحصيل ، ولمْ يَقُلْ : بحيثُ يحصلُ مثلاً ؛ ليشمل الفكر الفاسد صورةً أو مادَّةٌ، وأما الحدس - وهو حصول المطلوب مع مباديه دفعةً - فَخَارج بقيدِ الملاحظة، كما قالَ الدَّوَانيُّ : إنَّ المراد بالملاحظة هوَ التَّوجه نحو المعلوم قصداً كما نبه عليه السياق؛ سيَّما وقدْ قُيّدَ ،بالغاية، فإنَّها لا تكونُ إلَّا لِمَا هو حاصل بالاختيار؛ فلا نقضَ بالحدس؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ بِقَصْدِ النَّفْسِ واختيارها؛ بل يسنحُ بغير اختيار؛ إما عقيبَ شَوقٍ أو بدونه .ا.هـ.
By the mentioned definition it is comprehensive, as al-Jalal said: All individuals of reflection without exception, whether with simple or compound, known or conjectured, or unknown by compound ignorance, etc. It was objected to the claim of its comprehensiveness for the simple that the observation is not absolute observation of intelligibles; rather it is observation of intelligibles occurring within the two movements and ordering; so the author's definition does not apply to the simple either. Then the author defined reflection in the second section of this book containing theology by the two movements because reflection on the simple does not occur in the discussions of theology; so there is no need to include it in the definition. But here, it is needed because the principles of the art must be general. And from the coldness is the statement of some marginalia: it is likely that what is here is an indication that he intended from the sum of the two movements there the observation mentioned here. (His statement: 'to acquire the unknown' – the lam is for purpose; i.e., the observation whose motive is acquisition. So the single premise is excluded because ordering in it is not for acquiring the unknown but for acquiring the premise. And the equality syllogism and implication enter via contrapositive, although they exclude them from syllogism because they restricted implication there to being from the essence of the syllogism, and there is no necessity in them by essence. Also reflection on the second proof after the first proof enters because the intended from it is knowledge of the aspect of its indication, which is unknown. And he only said 'to acquire' and did not say 'such that it acquires' for example, to include corrupt thinking in form or matter. As for intuition – which is the attainment of the sought together with its principles at once – it is excluded by the restriction of observation, as al-Dawani said: 'The meaning of observation is the intentional direction toward the known as the context suggested, especially since it is restricted by the goal, for it is only for what occurs by choice. So there is no objection by intuition because it is not by the soul's intention and choice; rather it occurs involuntarily, either after desire or without it.' etc.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of nazar (reflective thinking) given by the matn author. It begins by affirming that the definition covers all instances of nazar, as al-Jalal stated, including those that are simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab), known, conjectured, or even ignorantly unknown. However, an objection is raised: the observation (ملاحظة) in the definition is not absolute but specifically the observation of intelligibles within two movements and ordering, so it does not apply to simple reflection. The commentator then clarifies that in the earlier section (theology), nazar was defined with the two movements precisely because simple reflection does not occur there; but here in logic, the rules must be general, so the definition must potentially include the simple. The commentator criticizes a marginal note that claims the text here is a clue that the author meant the same observation as in theology, calling it 'cold' (weak). Next, the phrase 'to acquire the unknown' is explained: the lam is for purpose, meaning the observation is motivated by acquisition. This excludes cases where ordering is not for acquiring the unknown, such as ordering a single premise to acquire that premise itself. It also includes certain types of reasoning like equality syllogism (qiyas al-musawat) and implication via contrapositive, even if some exclude them from the definition of qiyas. Also included is reflection on a second proof after a first, because the knowledge of the aspect of indication is unknown. The commentator notes that the author used 'لتحصيل' (to acquire) rather than 'بحيث يحصل' (such that it acquires) to include invalid thinking (fasad sura or madda). Finally, intuition (hads) is excluded by the restriction of observation: hads is the sudden attainment of the sought with its principles, without deliberate intention and choice. Al-Dawani is quoted to emphasize that observation requires intentional direction and choice, unlike hads which occurs involuntarily.

Key Terms
النظر
الملاحظة
الحدس
لتحصيل المجهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the lam in 'لتحصيل المجهول' according to the commentator?
A) To include single premises in the definition.
B) To exclude single premises and to include invalid thinking.
C) To exclude invalid thinking and include intuition.
D) To emphasize that observation must be with choice.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is defined as intentional, ordered observation for acquiring the unknown, excluding both accidental intuition and single-step comprehension.

Scope of Nazar: Inclusion and Exclusion
Author discusses what nazar includes and excludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish what is included in nazar (mufrad, invalid thinking, second proof) from what is excluded (single premise, hads) and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The definition intentionally includes flawed reasoning and excludes involuntary intuition.

After defining nazar, the commentator clarifies its scope. The definition includes simple reflection (mufrad) and even corrupt thinking, but excludes intuition (hads) and the single premise case. This ensures that nazar covers all forms of deliberate, ordered reasoning, even if erroneous, while separating it from sudden insight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two exclusions
Find where the commentator excludes the single premise (المقدمة الواحدة) and intuition (الحدس). Understand the reasons: single premise is excluded because its ordering is not for acquiring unknown but for the premise itself; intuition is excluded because it lacks intentional observation.
Exclusion is based on the condition of 'for acquiring unknown' and 'observation'.
2
Understand the inclusion of corrupt thinking
The commentator notes that the author used 'لتحصيل' (to acquire) rather than 'بحيث يحصل' (such that it acquires) to include corrupt thinking in form or matter. This means even invalid syllogisms are nazar as long as they aim to acquire unknown.
This shows that nazar is defined by intention and process, not correctness.
3
Examine the debate on simple (mufrad)
Some object that mufrad does not involve two movements, so it's not covered. The commentator responds that logically it should be included for generality, though in theology it's not used. He criticizes a marginal note that equates the two-movement definition with the observation here.
This reveals a tension between logical generality and disciplinary specificity.
