This passage is a commentary on the definition of nazar (reflective thinking) given by the matn author. It begins by affirming that the definition covers all instances of nazar, as al-Jalal stated, including those that are simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab), known, conjectured, or even ignorantly unknown. However, an objection is raised: the observation (ملاحظة) in the definition is not absolute but specifically the observation of intelligibles within two movements and ordering, so it does not apply to simple reflection. The commentator then clarifies that in the earlier section (theology), nazar was defined with the two movements precisely because simple reflection does not occur there; but here in logic, the rules must be general, so the definition must potentially include the simple. The commentator criticizes a marginal note that claims the text here is a clue that the author meant the same observation as in theology, calling it 'cold' (weak). Next, the phrase 'to acquire the unknown' is explained: the lam is for purpose, meaning the observation is motivated by acquisition. This excludes cases where ordering is not for acquiring the unknown, such as ordering a single premise to acquire that premise itself. It also includes certain types of reasoning like equality syllogism (qiyas al-musawat) and implication via contrapositive, even if some exclude them from the definition of qiyas. Also included is reflection on a second proof after a first, because the knowledge of the aspect of indication is unknown. The commentator notes that the author used 'لتحصيل' (to acquire) rather than 'بحيث يحصل' (such that it acquires) to include invalid thinking (fasad sura or madda). Finally, intuition (hads) is excluded by the restriction of observation: hads is the sudden attainment of the sought with its principles, without deliberate intention and choice. Al-Dawani is quoted to emphasize that observation requires intentional direction and choice, unlike hads which occurs involuntarily.

Key Terms
النظر
المفرد
المركب
الحدس
المقدمة الواحدة
الفكر الفاسد

The Second Movement: Why Intuition Is Not Reflection
Mir Ghiyath objects to al-Dawani's exclusion of hads; author responds with a new argument about the second movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the absence of the second movement (ordering) excludes intuition from reflection (nazar) in the Islamic logical tradition, and evaluate objections to this distinction.

Core Idea
Intuition gains knowledge without deliberate ordering; nazar requires the second movement.

In this passage, we witness a dialectical exchange between Mir Ghiyath and the author (al-Dasuqi). Mir Ghiyath challenges al-Dawani's exclusion of intuition (hads) from reflection (nazar), arguing that the criteria of intention and choice do not differentiate them. The author responds with a decisive refinement: the second movement—the deliberate ordering that produces the conclusion—is absent in intuition. This insight resolves the objection and clarifies the definition of nazar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objector's Argument
Mir Ghiyath attacks al-Dawani's distinction by offering two interpretations of 'intention and choice'. First, if he means that the principles in hads are not obtained intentionally, then in nazar they are also often obtained involuntarily (many principles are a priori). Second, if he means that attention to principles is intentional in nazar but not in hads, then that is denied—no real difference exists.
The objector forces the opponent to clarify the criterion.
2
Extract the Author's Refinement
The author introduces a new point: the second movement (ordering) is absent in hads. While the first movement (transition from sought to principles) can occur involuntarily, the second movement (deliberate arrangement to produce the conclusion) is unique to nazar. Thus, definitions of nazar that involve two movements or ordering are not vulnerable to Mir Ghiyath's objection.
The key shift: from intention/choice to structural necessity.
3
Apply the Refinement to Definitions of Nazar
The author shows that definitions using 'two movements' or 'ordering' are safe because ordering is necessary for the second movement. As for the definition by 'observation' (ملاحظة), the lam lila ajl (purpose) already indicates that the sought is posterior to the intelligibles reflected on—unlike intuition. Thus, intuition is excluded.
The lam lila ajl implies a causal ordering that intuition lacks.
4
Respond to the Commentator's Objection
The commentator (al-Muhashi) raises a scenario: the definition could apply to the first movement alone when both movements are present, or to observation of already-ordered principles. The author rebuts: the first movement alone does not produce the ordering; the definition is satisfied only when observation is restricted by the purpose (lam lila ajl), which requires the second movement.
This shows the commentator's objection is deflected by the purpose condition.

And Mir Ghiyath objected to him that if he means that the occurrence of principles in the mind in the case of intuition is not by intention and choice, then their occurrence in the case of reflection is often likewise, how not, and most of its principles are a priori matters that we do not know how they occurred? And if he means that the direction and attention to the principles occurring in the case of reflection is by intention and choice unlike intuition, then it is denied, and no difference appears between the two cases at all, as is not hidden from anyone with grasp. (I say): It appeared to me when teaching this place that the second movement does not exist in intuition, only the first; so there is no objection against those who define reflection by the two movements or by ordering. As for the first, it is obvious; as for the second, because I know that ordering is necessary for the second movement. As for its occurrence against the definition of reflection by observation, it is supposed, and its refutation is by making the lam for purpose as we said, because then its object is a consequent cause, and therein is an indication that the sought to be acquired is posterior to the intelligibles reflected on, which is not the case for intuition. Also, the meaning of 'observation of intelligibles' is the observation occurring within the transition from the sought to the principles, then from them to it, as al-Meybudi explicitly stated in his Sharh al-Tawali'. The commentator said: Also an objection was raised against the definition that it applies to the first movement in the scenario of the sum of the two movements even though reflection is the sum in this scenario by agreement, and it applies to the observation of ordered principles known previously, as when the laughing body is known with this order previously, so the soul observes it for the acquisition of [the knowledge of] man, and no one said that there is thinking without order except in the case of reflection on the simple, and that there is no difference between it and the simple. (I say): There is no objection. As for the first scenario, because the first movement, as you have heard, produces the principles of the sought, and their occurrence in the soul only leads after the ordering achieved by the second movement. So after the observation is restricted by the purpose as we said in the exclusion of intuition, the definition does not apply to this scenario. As for defining it by the sum of the two movements or...

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. Mir Ghiyath objects to al-Dawani's claim that hads (intuition) is excluded from nazar because nazar requires intention and choice. He argues that if al-Dawani means that in hads the principles are not attained intentionally, then in nazar they are also often attained involuntarily (since many principles are a priori). If al-Dawani means that the attention to principles is intentional in nazar but not in hads, then that is denied—there is no difference. The author (likely al-Dasuqi) responds with his own insight: the key is that the second movement (حركة ثانية) is absent in hads. Intuition only has the first movement (attaining the principles), but reflection requires both movements. Thus, definitions of nazar as two movements or as ordering (which implies the second movement) are not vulnerable to this objection. For definitions by observation, the lam lila ajl (purpose) already indicates that the sought is posterior to the reflections, unlike intuition. The author then mentions another objection raised by the commentator (al-Muhashi): the definition could apply to the first movement alone in a scenario where both movements are present, or to observation of previously known ordered principles. The author refutes this, explaining that the first movement alone does not produce the ordering; the definition is satisfied only when observation is restricted by the purpose (lam lila ajl), which requires the second movement. The text cuts off mid-sentence while discussing the definition by sum of two movements.

Sawab and Khata' in Actions vs Judgments
Al-Dasuqi explains sawab/khata' for actions and uses rational contradiction to argue thought is fallible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sawab/khata' for judgments (correspondence to reality) and for actions (conformity to purpose).

Core Idea
Sawab in action means effective method, not just true conclusion.

Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the terms 'correct' and 'error' have different meanings when applied to judgments versus actions. In logic textbooks, sawab often means truth, but here it is broadened to include practical effectiveness. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating whether a thought is properly structured to achieve its purpose: leading to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts
Al-Dasuqi states that sawab/khata' are attributed to both judgments (hukm) and actions (fi'l). Read the Arabic and note the shift in meaning.
Look for the phrase 'تارةً يُوصفُ بهما الحكم' and 'تارة يوصفُ بهما الفعلُ'.
2
Extract the definition for judgments
For judgments, sawab means correspondence to reality (al-waqi'), and khata' means non-correspondence. This is the classical truth condition.
Relate this to the concept of hukm being true or false.
3
Extract the definition for actions
For actions, sawab means conformity to the purpose (al-gharad). The action's correctness is measured by its effectiveness in achieving its goal.
The purpose of thought is to lead to tasawwur or tasdiq.
4
Apply to thought as an action
Thought (fikr) is considered an action. For it to be sawab, it must meet conditions: for conceptualization, proper arrangement of genus before differentia; for assent, a first-figure syllogism with affirmative minor and universal major.
List these conditions from the Arabic: 'كأن يقع الجنس مقدماً على الفصل' and 'كأن تكون الصغرى موجبة، والكبرى كلية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَمُ أنَّ الصَّواب ضد الخطأ، ثمَّ تارةً يُوصفُ بهما الحكم، وحينئذ يكون المرادُ بالصَّوابِ مُطابقة الحكم للواقع، وبالخطأ عدم مطابقته للواقع، وتارة يوصفُ بهما الفعلُ كَمَا هنا، وحينئذ ؛ يكون المراد بالصَّوابِ موافقة الفعل للغرض، وبالخطأ عدمَ مُطابقتِهِ للغرض، فمعنى كون الفكر صواباً ؛ أَنَّهُ مُوافق للغرض بأنْ يكونَ مُستجمعاً لِلشُّروط ؛ كأن يقع الجنس مقدَّماً على الفصل في ترتيب القول الشارح الموصل للتَّصورِ ، وكأن تكون الصُّغرى موجبة، والكُبرى كُلِّيَّةٌ في ترتيب قياس من الشَّكلِ الأوَّلِ موصل للتصديق، ومعنى كونه ليس بصواب؛ أنَّه لم يكن موافقاً للغرض ؛ لكونه لم يحتو على الشروط كلّها . (قَوْلُهُ : كَيْفَ ... إلخ) المقصود من هذا الاستفهام؛ التَّعجُبُ مِن قولهم: إِنَّ الفكر صواب دائماً المنفى بقولِهِ : لأنَّ الفكر ليس بصواب دائماً، وحينئذ ؛ فالمستفهم عنه المتعجب منه محذوف، وقوله : وقد يُناقض جملة حاليَّة؛ أي: كيف يتوهم أنَّ الفكر صواب دائماً ، والحالُ أنَّه قد يُناقض ؛ أَي : إِنَّهُ يُتعجَّبُ مِن التَّوهُم تلكَ الحالة؛ إذْ  لوْ كانَ الفكر صواباً ؛ مَا تناقض العقلاء تناقضُوا ، فتناقضُهُم يدلُّ على أنَّ الفكر ليسَ صواباً دائماً . والحاصلُ : أَنَّهُ يُتعجب من كون الفكرِ صواباً دائماً مع وجودِ ما يدلُّ على أَنَّهُ ليس بصواب دائماً ، وهو تناقض العقلاء.
And know that correct (sawab) is the opposite of error (khata'). Sometimes they are attributed to judgment (hukm), in which case correct means correspondence of the judgment to reality, and error means non-correspondence. Sometimes they are attributed to an action (fi'l) as here, in which case correct means the action's conformity to the purpose, and error means non-conformity to the purpose. So the meaning of thought being correct is that it conforms to the purpose by encompassing the conditions: such as the genus being placed before the differentia in the arrangement of the explicative statement that leads to conceptualization, and such as the minor premise being affirmative and the major premise universal in the arrangement of a syllogism of the first figure that leads to assent. And the meaning of it not being correct is that it does not conform to the purpose because it does not encompass all conditions. (His statement: 'How... etc.') The purpose of this interrogative is amazement at their saying: 'thought is always correct,' which is negated by his statement: 'because thought is not always correct.' Therefore, the object of amazement is omitted. His statement: 'and yet it may contradict' is a circumstantial clause; i.e., how can one imagine that thought is always correct while it may contradict? That is, amazement at that imagination; because if thought were always correct, rational people would not contradict each other. Their contradiction indicates that thought is not always correct. The upshot: one is amazed at thought being always correct while there exists what indicates that it is not always correct, namely the contradiction of rational people.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Dasuqi on two aspects. First, he clarifies the terms 'correct' (sawab) and 'error' (khata') when applied to actions versus judgments. When applied to judgments, correctness means correspondence to reality. When applied to actions (as here, since thought is an action of the mind), correctness means conformity to the purpose (gharaḍ). For thought, the purpose is to lead to knowledge: either conceptualization (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). The conditions for correct thought are: for conceptualization, the proper arrangement of genus (jins) before differentia (fasl) in a definition; for assent, a syllogism in the first figure with an affirmative minor premise and a universal major premise. If these conditions are met, thought is 'correct' in the sense of being effective. If not, it is 'error' as non-conformity to the purpose. Second, he explains the matn's rhetorical question 'كيف وقد يناقض' (How, while it may contradict?). He interprets this as an expression of amazement at the claim that thought is always correct. The evidence of error is the observed fact that rational people contradict each other. If thought were always correct, there would be no disagreement; ergo, thought is not infallible. This is an inductive argument from observed contradiction to the fallibility of thought.

Key Terms
الصواب (al-ṣawāb)
الخطأ (al-khaṭa')
الغرض (al-gharaḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is free from contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Sawab for actions is about effective method, not just true correspondence; it is measured by fulfillment of purpose.

The Inductive Argument from Contradiction to Fallibility
Al-Dasuqi explains sawab/khata' for actions and uses rational contradiction to argue thought is fallible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Dasuqi's inductive argument that thought is not always correct, based on observed contradiction among rational people.

Core Idea
Observed disagreement proves human thought is fallible.

Al-Dasuqi uses a simple but powerful observation: rational people contradict each other. If thought were always correct, such contradiction would be impossible. Therefore, thought is not infallible. This is an inductive argument from many instances to a general conclusion about the nature of human reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The unnamed opponents claim that thought is always correct (sawab). This is the absolute claim that al-Dasuqi attacks.
Look for 'قولهم: إن الفكر صواب دائماً'.
2
Find the evidence
Al-Dasuqi points to the fact that rational people ('uqala') contradict each other (tanāquḍ). This is an observed phenomenon.
The phrase 'تناقض العقلاء' is key.
3
Understand the logical connection
If thought were always correct, then all thinkers would arrive at the same correct conclusions. Since they do not (contradiction occurs), thought cannot be always correct. This is a modus tollens: if always correct, then no contradiction; contradiction exists; therefore not always correct.
The assumption is that correct thought leads to agreement.
4
Recognize the inductive nature
The evidence is empirical and based on multiple instances of disagreement. It is not a deductive proof but an inductive generalization from observed contradictions to the fallibility of thought.
The argument is strengthened by the universality of the observation across cultures and times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلَمُ أنَّ الصَّواب ضد الخطأ، ثمَّ تارةً يُوصفُ بهما الحكم، وحينئذ يكون المرادُ بالصَّوابِ مُطابقة الحكم للواقع، وبالخطأ عدم مطابقته للواقع، وتارة يوصفُ بهما الفعلُ كَمَا هنا، وحينئذ ؛ يكون المراد بالصَّوابِ موافقة الفعل للغرض، وبالخطأ عدمَ مُطابقتِهِ للغرض، فمعنى كون الفكر صواباً ؛ أَنَّهُ مُوافق للغرض بأنْ يكونَ مُستجمعاً لِلشُّروط ؛ كأن يقع الجنس مقدَّماً على الفصل في ترتيب القول الشارح الموصل للتَّصورِ ، وكأن تكون الصُّغرى موجبة، والكُبرى كُلِّيَّةٌ في ترتيب قياس من الشَّكلِ الأوَّلِ موصل للتصديق، ومعنى كونه ليس بصواب؛ أنَّه لم يكن موافقاً للغرض ؛ لكونه لم يحتو على الشروط كلّها . (قَوْلُهُ : كَيْفَ ... إلخ) المقصود من هذا الاستفهام؛ التَّعجُبُ مِن قولهم: إِنَّ الفكر صواب دائماً المنفى بقولِهِ : لأنَّ الفكر ليس بصواب دائماً، وحينئذ ؛ فالمستفهم عنه المتعجب منه محذوف، وقوله : وقد يُناقض جملة حاليَّة؛ أي: كيف يتوهم أنَّ الفكر صواب دائماً ، والحالُ أنَّه قد يُناقض ؛ أَي : إِنَّهُ يُتعجَّبُ مِن التَّوهُم تلكَ الحالة؛ إذْ  لوْ كانَ الفكر صواباً ؛ مَا تناقض العقلاء تناقضُوا ، فتناقضُهُم يدلُّ على أنَّ الفكر ليسَ صواباً دائماً . والحاصلُ : أَنَّهُ يُتعجب من كون الفكرِ صواباً دائماً مع وجودِ ما يدلُّ على أَنَّهُ ليس بصواب دائماً ، وهو تناقض العقلاء.
And know that correct (sawab) is the opposite of error (khata'). Sometimes they are attributed to judgment (hukm), in which case correct means correspondence of the judgment to reality, and error means non-correspondence. Sometimes they are attributed to an action (fi'l) as here, in which case correct means the action's conformity to the purpose, and error means non-conformity to the purpose. So the meaning of thought being correct is that it conforms to the purpose by encompassing the conditions: such as the genus being placed before the differentia in the arrangement of the explicative statement that leads to conceptualization, and such as the minor premise being affirmative and the major premise universal in the arrangement of a syllogism of the first figure that leads to assent. And the meaning of it not being correct is that it does not conform to the purpose because it does not encompass all conditions. (His statement: 'How... etc.') The purpose of this interrogative is amazement at their saying: 'thought is always correct,' which is negated by his statement: 'because thought is not always correct.' Therefore, the object of amazement is omitted. His statement: 'and yet it may contradict' is a circumstantial clause; i.e., how can one imagine that thought is always correct while it may contradict? That is, amazement at that imagination; because if thought were always correct, rational people would not contradict each other. Their contradiction indicates that thought is not always correct. The upshot: one is amazed at thought being always correct while there exists what indicates that it is not always correct, namely the contradiction of rational people.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Dasuqi on two aspects. First, he clarifies the terms 'correct' (sawab) and 'error' (khata') when applied to actions versus judgments. When applied to judgments, correctness means correspondence to reality. When applied to actions (as here, since thought is an action of the mind), correctness means conformity to the purpose (gharaḍ). For thought, the purpose is to lead to knowledge: either conceptualization (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). The conditions for correct thought are: for conceptualization, the proper arrangement of genus (jins) before differentia (fasl) in a definition; for assent, a syllogism in the first figure with an affirmative minor premise and a universal major premise. If these conditions are met, thought is 'correct' in the sense of being effective. If not, it is 'error' as non-conformity to the purpose. Second, he explains the matn's rhetorical question 'كيف وقد يناقض' (How, while it may contradict?). He interprets this as an expression of amazement at the claim that thought is always correct. The evidence of error is the observed fact that rational people contradict each other. If thought were always correct, there would be no disagreement; ergo, thought is not infallible. This is an inductive argument from observed contradiction to the fallibility of thought.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ)
التعجب (al-ta'ajjub)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key evidence al-Dasuqi uses to argue that thought is not always correct?
A) The complexity of logical rules.
B) The existence of contradiction among rational people.
C) The impossibility of defining 'correct'.
D) The need for divine guidance.

Key Takeaway
The observed fact that rational people contradict each other provides inductive evidence that human thought is not infallible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another instance in Islamic thought where observed disagreement is used to argue against an absolute claim. For example, the disagreement among qira'at readers on certain recitations: does that prove that no single recitation is absolutely correct? Discuss using al-Dasuqi's framework.

Definition by Goal: Why It Is a Description (Rasm)
Dasuqi and Attar explain why definition by goal is a description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential definition (hadd) and descriptive definition (rasm) by identifying whether the definiens is internal or external to the essence.

Core Idea
Defining by goal is a description because the goal is external to what is defined.

In logic, not every definition captures the essence. Some describe by external properties. This passage clarifies that defining knowledge by its goal—the need for logic—is a description (rasm), not a full essential definition (hadd). Understanding this difference is key to evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
What is being defined here? The subject of logic, i.e., knowledge (‘ilm). Note that the definition emerges from clarifying the need (haja).
2
Distinguish hadd vs rasm
An essential definition (hadd) uses genus and differentia; a descriptive definition (rasm) uses external properties like cause, effect, or goal. Here, the goal (ghāya) is external to the essence of knowledge.
The commentators stress that the goal is a property (khāṣṣa), so definition by it is rasm.
3
Verify why goal is external
The goal (safeguarding the intellect) is an effect or purpose, not a part of the essence. As al-Dasuqi says: 'the goal of something is external to it.'
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, defining 'ijma' as 'agreement of mujtahids' is a rasm because agreement is an external property. A true hadd would include genus and differentia.
Practice recognizing rasm in other definitions, e.g., 'fiqh is knowledge of practical rulings'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المستلزم لتعريف العِلمِ برَسْمِه؛ إذ يُعلَمُ مِن بيان الحاجة غايةُ العِلْم، والتَّعريفُ بالغايةِ رَسُمٌ; فَلِذَا أَدْرَجَ المصنِّفُ التَّعريف في بيانِ الحاجة كما سيجيءُ. والحاصل: إنَّ العِلم: إمَّا تصوُّرٌ ساذج، أو تصديق. وكلُّ واحدٍ منَ التَّصور والتّصديقِ ينقسمُ بحَسَبِ الضَّرورة إلى: الضَّروريّ، والكسبي.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: إِذْ يُعْلَمُ) علَّةٌ لِقولِهِ: المستلزم. (قَوْلُهُ: غَايَةُ الْعِلْم) إِنَّما كانت غايةٌ لعلم معلومة من بيان الحاجة؛ لأنَّ الغاية والحاجة مُتَّحدانِ ذاتاً، وإنَّما يختلفان اعتباراً، فالصِّفةُ المذكورة من حيث كونها نهاية هذا العِلْم يُقال لها: غاية، وَمِنْ حيثُ إِنَّها مُحتاج إليها؛ يُقالُ لَهَا: حاجة. (قَوْلُهُ: رَشَمٌ) أي: لأنَّ غايةَ الشَّيء خارجةٌ عنه، والتّعريفُ بالخارج رَسم. (قَوْلُهُ: فَلِذَا) أي: فَلِأجل أنَّ بيان الحاجة مستلزم للتَّعريف. (قَوْلُهُ: كَمَا سَيَجِيءُ) أي: التنبيه على الإدراج المذكور؛ حيثُ قالَ الشَّارِحُ: (فيما سيأتي هذا تعريفٌ للمنطق المندرج في بيان الحاجة). (قَوْلُهُ: وَالْحَاصِلُ) أي: حاصلُ بيانِ الحاجةِ الَّذي أشار له المصنِّفُ بقولِهِ: العِلْمُ إِنْ كَانَ إذعاناً ... إلخ.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: المُسْتَلْزِمُ) مرفوع؛ صفةٌ للبيان، وقوله: (إذ يعلمُ ... إلخ)؛ تعليل لقوله: المستلزم. (قَوْلُهُ: غَايَةُ الْعِلْم) أي: ثمرتُهُ المترتِّبةُ عليه، وقوله: (والتَّعريفُ بالغاية رسم)؛ لأنَّ غايةَ الشَّيءِ خاصَّةٌ مِن خواصه، والتّعريفُ بِالخاصَّةِ رسم. (قَوْلُهُ: وَالْحَاصِلُ ... إلخ) إجمالٌ لِلكلامِ السَّابق ليربط به قوله: فاحتيج، وليظهر فائدة التفريع بالفاء.
It necessitates defining knowledge by its description; for from the clarification of the need the goal of knowledge is known, and definition by goal is a description. Therefore the author included the definition in the clarification of the need, as will come. The summary: knowledge is either simple conception or assent. And each of conception and assent divides, in terms of necessity, into the necessary and the acquired.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'for it is known') is a cause for his statement: 'necessitates'. (His statement: 'the goal of knowledge') It is only a goal of knowledge known from the clarification of the need because the goal and the need are identical in essence, differing only in consideration; the mentioned attribute, in terms of being the end of this science, is called a goal, and in terms of being needed, is called a need. (His statement: 'a description') i.e., because the goal of something is external to it, and definition by the external is a description. (His statement: 'therefore') i.e., because the clarification of the need necessitates the definition. (His statement: 'as will come') i.e., alerting to the mentioned inclusion; where the commentator said: 'Later this is a definition of logic included in the clarification of the need'. (His statement: 'the summary') i.e., the summary of the clarification of the need to which the author alluded by his statement: 'Knowledge, if it is assent...' etc.
Al-'Attar: (His statement: 'necessitates') is nominative, an adjective for 'clarification', and his statement: ('for it is known' etc.) is a cause for his statement: 'necessitates'. (His statement: 'the goal of knowledge') i.e., its resulting fruit. (And his statement: 'definition by goal is a description') because the goal of a thing is a property among its properties, and definition by property is a description. (His statement: 'the summary' etc.) is a summary of the previous speech to connect with his statement: 'so we need', and to show the benefit of the deduction with 'fa'.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage explains how clarifying the need for logic leads to a definition of the science (knowledge) by its goal. The author states that the clarification of need necessarily implies a definition of knowledge by description (rasm), because from the need we know the goal (ghaya), and defining by goal is a type of descriptive definition. Al-Dasuqi elaborates that the goal and the need are identical in essence but differ in aspect: the safeguard is a goal relative to the science, and a need relative to the seeker. He emphasizes that definition by the external (goal) is a rasm, not a hadd. The passage then summarizes the content of knowledge: it is either simple conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq), each of which is either necessary (daruri, self-evident) or acquired (kasbi). Al-'Attar clarifies the grammatical status of 'necessitates' and reiterates that the goal is a property, hence definition by it is a rasm. The summary serves to connect the preceding discussion to the upcoming need for a canon. This establishes the foundational epistemological division that underlies the entire science of logic.

Key Terms
رسم
غاية
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is definition by goal considered a rasm?
A) Because the goal is the most important part
B) Because the goal is external to the essence
C) Because the goal is unknown
D) Because the goal is internal

Key Takeaway
Definition by goal is a type of rasm because the goal is a property external to the essence of the defined thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the definition of logic in the beginning of al-Akhdari: 'Logic is a tool that safeguards the mind from error.' Is this a hadd or a rasm? Explain why.

The Twofold Division of Knowledge: Conception and Assent
The passage summarizes the division of knowledge into conception and assent, each necessary or acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any piece of knowledge as either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq), and each as necessary (daruri) or acquired (kasbi).

Core Idea
Knowledge is either simple conception or assent, and each is either self-evident or acquired.

The passage provides a foundational summary: all knowledge divides into two types—conception (mere understanding of a concept) and assent (judgment about a proposition). Each of these is either necessary (self-evident) or acquired (requiring reasoning). This classification underpins the entire methodology of logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two primary divisions
Conception (tasawwur) is the simple grasp of a meaning without judgment; assent (tasdiq) is affirming or denying a proposition.
2
Identify the secondary division
Each of these branches into necessary (daruri) and acquired (kasbi). Necessary is known without effort; acquired is reached through reasoning.
3
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take the conception of 'salat': your understanding of what salat means is a conception. Is it necessary or acquired? Most people acquire it. In contrast, the belief that 'salat is obligatory' is an assent, and its necessity varies.
4
Distinguish between the divisions
Conception and assent are mutually exclusive and exhaustive of knowledge. Similarly, necessary and acquired apply within each, but not across. A conception can be necessary (e.g., innate concepts) or acquired.

Mu'arraf and Qawl Sharah: The Near Definer
Defining mu'arraf and qawl sharah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the near definer (mu'arraf) from other leads and explain why mu'arraf and qawl sharah are synonymous.

Core Idea
A mu'arraf is not just any definer—it is the near lead that directly brings the essence to mind.

This passage introduces the central terms of definition in logic: mu'arraf and qawl sharah. You will learn how they are defined and why they are restricted to the 'near lead' that directly conveys conceptual knowledge, unlike far or farthest leads.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage begins by stating that logic deals with conceptual and assertoric knowledge. Mu'arraf is introduced as what leads to a conceptual demand.
Notice the two types of knowledge: tasawwuri (conceptual) and tasdiqi (assertoric).
2
Parse the key terms
Note that mu'arraf is equated with qawl sharah. The commentator clarifies that 'mu'arraf' here means the near lead (al-muwasil al-qarib), not any lead.
Al-muwasil al-qarib is the direct definer, e.g., the definition (hadd).
3
Understand the nuance
The commentator distinguishes this from the absolute lead (muwasil mutlaq), which includes far (ba'id) and farthest (ab'ad). So mu'arraf is specifically the definition or description that directly explains the essence.
This restriction is crucial: not every lead qualifies as mu'arraf.
4
Examine the term qawl sharah
It is called 'qawl' because it is usually composed, and 'sharah' because it explains the essence either fully (bi al-kunh, i.e., hadd) or partially (bi al-wajh, i.e., rasm).
gawl sharah is thus a synonym for mu'arraf.
5
Appreciate the expansion
The passage notes that although technically only hadd fully explains essence, scholars extended 'qawl sharah' to all definitions due to relation.
This shows how technical terms can broaden in usage.

مَوْضُوعُ عِلْمٍ الْمَنْطِقِ ... تَصَوُّرِي كالإنسانِ مثلاً، فَيُسَمَّى ذلك الموصل إلى المطلوب التصوري مُعَرِّفاً، وقولاً شارحاً. - (أَوْ) من حيثُ إِنَّ ذلك المعلومَ التَّصديقيَّ يُوصِلُ إلى مطلوب تَصْدِيقِي، كَقولنا : «العالم حادث مثلاً، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : مَثَلاً لا حاجة لهُ معَ الكافِ؛ إلا أن تكون إحداهما لإدخال الأفراد الخارجية، والأُخرى لإدخال الأفراد الذهنية . (قَوْلُهُ : فَيُسَمَّى مُعَرِّفَاً) ضميرُ يُسمَّى عائدٌ على المعلوم التصوري الموصل، لكن لا بالمعنى السابق، وهو مُطلقُ الموصل الصَّادقِ بالقريب والبعيدِ والأَبْعَدِ، بل بمعنى الموصل القريبِ كَالحد فيكون في كلامه استخدام لا شبهه كَمَا قِيْلَ. (قَوْلُهُ : مُعَرِّفَاً) إِنّما سُمِّيَ مُعرِّفاً ؛ لتعريفه المخاطب الماهية. (قَوْلُهُ: وَقَوْلَاً شَارِحَاً إِنَّما سُمِّيَ قَولاً ؛ لأنَّه في الغالبِ مُركَّب، فالقولُ يُرادفُهُ، وأَمَّا تسميته شارحاً ؛ فَلِشرحِهِ الماهيَّةَ؛ إما بالكُنهِ أو بالوجه، قيل : إِنَّ تسميته قولاً شارحاً من تسمية الشَّيء باسم بعض أفرادِهِ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يشرحُ الماهية إلا ذاتياتها، فلا يكونُ القولُ الشَّارِحُ إِلَّا حدا باعتبار الأصل، لكن أطلقوا على التعاريف أنَّها جميع قول شارخ لهذه العلاقة . وهذا إنْ أُريد بشرح الماهِيَّةِ بيانُ أجزائها الخاصَّةِ بها، وأَمَّا إِنْ أُريدَ بِهَا مَا يشمل تمييزها عن غيرها ؛ لم يكن هذا من باب تسمية الشَّيء باسم بعض أفراده. (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مِنْ حَيْثُ . . . إلخ) «أو» بمعنى الواو . (قَوْلُهُ : مَثَلَا) فيه ما مرَّ . العطار وإلى المعلومِ التصديقي بالنِّسبة إلى المطلوبِ التَّصديقي ؛ وهو يقتضي خروج البحث عن المعلوم التصوري مِن حيثُ الإيصال إلى المطلوب التصديقي، وعَنِ المعلوم التصديقي من حيثُ الإيصال إلى المطلوبِ التَّصوري، وهذا مبني على مَا هُوَ الحق مِن مَنْع اكتساب أحدِهِما مِنَ الآخَرِ ، ولَهُم هَهُنا كلامٌ مُتهَافِتٌ أعرضُنَا عَن تفتيشِهِ .
The subject of the science of logic is... a conceptual [knowledge] like 'human' for example, so that which leads to the conceptual demand is called a definer (mu'arraf) and an explanatory statement (qawl sharah). - Or from the aspect that that assertoric known leads to an assertoric demand, like our saying: 'The world is temporally originated' for example. al-Dusuqi: His saying: 'for example' has no need with the kaf, unless one of them is for entering external individuals and the other for entering mental individuals. His saying: 'so it is called a definer' – the pronoun of 'yusamma' refers to the conceptual known that leads, but not in the previous sense which is the absolute lead applying to the near, far, and farthest, but in the sense of the near lead like the definition (hadd), so in his speech there is a usage like it as was said. His saying: 'a definer' – it was only called a definer because it defines the essence to the addressee. His saying: 'and an explanatory statement' – it was only called a statement because it is mostly composed, so 'qawl' is synonymous; as for calling it explanatory, it is for explaining the essence either fully (bi al-kunh) or partially (bi al-wajh). It was said: calling it an explanatory statement is from naming a thing by the name of some of its individuals, because it does not explain the essence except its essentials, so the explanatory statement is only a definition according to the origin, but they applied all definitions as explanatory statements due to this relation. And if what is meant by explaining the essence is the statement of its specific parts, and if what is meant is what includes distinguishing it from others, then this is not from the chapter of naming a thing by the name of some of its individuals. His saying: 'Or from the aspect... etc.' – 'aw' is in the meaning of 'waw'. His saying: 'for example' – what passed applies to it. al-'Attar: And to the assertoric known in relation to the assertoric demand; and it necessitates the exit of the search from the conceptual known from the aspect of leading to the assertoric demand, and from the assertoric known from the aspect of leading to the conceptual demand, and this is built on what is correct from preventing acquisition of one from the other, and they have here contradictory speech which we refrained from examining.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines the terms mu'arraf and qawl sharah. It begins by stating the subject matter of logic as conceptual (tasawwuri) and assertoric (tasdiqi) knowledge. When a known thing leads to a conceptual demand (matlub tasawwuri), it is called a mu'arraf (definer) or qawl sharah (explanatory statement). The commentator al-Dusuqi clarifies that the pronoun in 'yusamma' refers specifically to the near lead (al-muwasil al-qarib), not any lead. Thus, mu'arraf is the near definer like the definition (hadd). He explains why it is called 'mu'arraf': because it defines the essence to the addressee. As for 'qawl sharah', it is called 'qawl' because it is usually a compound phrase, and 'sharah' because it explains the essence either fully (bi al-kunh, i.e., true definition) or partially (bi al-wajh, i.e., description). He also notes a nuance: technically only the definition (hadd) that includes essential parts truly explains the essence, but scholars extended the term 'qawl sharah' to all definitions by relation. The commentator al-'Attar then discusses the relation between conceptual and assertoric leads, emphasizing that the two types of knowledge are distinct and one cannot be acquired from the other, a point they leave without detailed examination.

Key Terms
مُعَرِّف (mu'arraf): The near lead (al-muwasil al-qarib) that brings about conceptual knowledge; it is specifically the definition or description that directly explains the essence. [Note: In earlier logic texts, mu'arraf sometimes referred to any definition; here it is restricted to the near definer.]
قَوْل شَارِح (qawl sharah): A statement that explains the essence either fully (true definition) or partially (description); it is synonymous with 'mu'arraf' in this context. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
مَوْصِل (muwasil): What leads or conveys to a demand; it can be near (qarib), far (ba'id), or farthest (ab'ad), but here only the near is meant. [Note: In later texts, 'muwasil' is used more generally for any means of knowledge.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the term 'mu'arraf' specifically refer to?
A) Any definition, whether near or far
B) The near lead (al-muwasil al-qarib) to conceptual knowledge
C) Only the definition (hadd) that includes essential parts
D) Any conceptual known

Key Takeaway
Mu'arraf and qawl sharah are synonymous terms for the near definer that directly brings the essence to mind, either fully or partially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example of a mu'arraf (near definer) for the concept of 'knowledge' in Islamic logic. Explain why it is near.
?
Identify whether 'a featherless biped' qualifies as a mu'arraf for 'human' according to this passage, and justify your answer.

The Primary Aim of Logic: Definitions and Proofs
Author defines hujja and dalil and concludes the primary aim of logic is these two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the primary aims of logic (definitions and proofs) from secondary topics (expressions and indications), and explain the etymological basis for the terms hujja and dalil.

Core Idea
The core of logic is near leads: definitions for concepts and proofs for assertions.

In this lesson, we explore how the author defines the central purpose of logic. He identifies two types of near leads (al-muwasil al-qarib) that directly deliver knowledge: definitions (for conceptuals) and proofs (for assertorics). These are the primary aim; other discussions, like expressions and indications, are merely tools. Understanding this hierarchy clarifies why we study logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms hujja and dalil
Find where the passage says 'that which leads to the assertoric demand is called a proof (hujja) and evidence (dalil)'. Note that both are defined as 'near leads' (al-muwasil al-qarib), not just any leads.
The 'near lead' means it directly produces knowledge without intermediary.
2
Understand the etymological reasoning
The passage explains that hujja is so named because one who uses it 'overcomes' (hajja) the opponent, i.e., defeats him. Dalil is named because it serves as evidence (yustadallu bihi). This shows that the names reflect their function in argumentation.
Etymology in logic often reveals the intended purpose of the term.
3
Distinguish primary vs. secondary aims
The author states that the primary aim (al-maqsud al-asli) of logic is 'encompassed' by definitions (for conceptuals) and proofs (for assertorics). Secondary aims like the study of expressions (alfaz) and indications (dalalat) are only means, not goals.
This distinction prevents confusion between tools and objectives in logic.
4
Recognize the five types of leads
Al-'Attar enumerates five types: near lead to conceptual (definitions), near lead to assertoric (proofs), far lead to conceptual (some of the five universals), far lead to assertoric (propositions), and farthest lead to assertoric (subjects, predicates, premises, consequents). This classification shows the scope of logic beyond the primary aim.
The near leads are the primary aim; the others are supportive.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَيُسَمَّى ذلك الموصل إلى المطلوب التصديقي (حُجَّةٌ)، ودليلاً. فانحصر المقصود الأصلي من هذا الفنّ الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَيُسَمَّى حُجَّةٌ) ضميرُ يُسمَّى عائدٌ على المعلوم التصديقي الموصل، لكن لا بالمعنى السَّابق وهو مُطلقُ الموصل الصَّادق بالقريب والبعيد، بل بمعنى الموصل القريب، ففي كلامه استخدام أيضاً . (قَوْلُهُ : حُجَّةٌ) إِنَّما سُمِّيَ حَجَّة ؛ لأنَّ مَنْ تمسَّكَ بِهِ في الاستدلال على مطلوبِهِ؛ حجَّ خصمه ؛ أي: غلبه. (قَوْلُهُ : وَدَلِيْلاً) إِنَّما سُمّي بذلك ؛ لأنَّه يُستدلُّ بِهِ على المطلوب. (قَوْلُهُ : فَانْحَصَرَ ... إلخ) تفريعٌ على ما سبق مِن أنَّ موضوعَهُ المعلومُ التَّصوري والتصديقي من حيثُ . . . إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ: الْمَقْصُوْدُ الْأَصْلِيُّ احترزَ بِهِ عن المقصودِ التَّبعي كمبحثِ الألفاظ (۱) والدَّلالات، فإنَّهما ليسا مقصودين بالذَّاتِ مِن فنّ المنطق، وإنَّما هما مقصودان بالتبع لتركب المعرف والقياس منهما . العطار (قَوْلُهُ : فَانْحَصَرَ المَقْصُوْدُ الأَصْلِيُّ ينبغي أن يعلم أوَّلاً أنَّ أقسام الموصل إلى التَّصوُّرِ أو التَّصديقِ المبحوث عنها في المنطق؛ خمسة : الموصل القريب إلى التَّصوُّرِ ؛ وهي المعرّفات والموصل القريبُ إلى التَّصديقاتِ ؛ وهِيَ الحجج، والموصل البعيدُ إلى التَّصوُّرِ ؛ وهو بعضُ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمس، والموصل البعيد إلى التصديقِ؛ وهو القضايا والموصلُ الأَبْعَدُ إليه ؛ وهو الموضوعات والمحمولات والمقدِّماتُ والتَّوالي، ولم يذكرُوا في الوصل إلى التَّصورِ موصلاً أَبْعَدَ، وفيه بحث (1) (قَوْلُهُ : كمبحث الألفاظ ... إلخ) الأولى أن يقول : كمبادئ التصورات وهي الكليات ومبادئ التصديقات وهي القضايا وأحكامها لأنها المقصودة بالتبع والمقصود بالذات مقاصد التَّصورات وهي الأقوال الشارحة ومقاصد التصديقات وهي الحجج يدلّ لذلك حصرهم الفنّ في هذا الأربعة. وأما مبحث الألفاظ والدَّلالات فليسا من الفن في شيء. قال السيد: الأولى أن يجعل مباحث الألفاظ أيضاً من المقدمة . ا.هـ. ويعني بها مقدمة الكتاب وأيضاً الدَّلالات. ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
So that which leads to the assertoric demand is called a proof (hujja) and evidence (dalil). Thus, the primary aim of this art is encompassed. al-Dusuqi: His saying: 'so it is called a proof' – the pronoun of 'yusamma' refers to the assertoric known that leads, but not in the previous sense which is the absolute lead applying to the near and far, but in the sense of the near lead, so in his speech there is also a usage. His saying: 'a proof' – it was only called a proof because whoever adheres to it in deducing his demand, he overcomes (hajja) his opponent, i.e., defeats him. His saying: 'and evidence' – it was only called that because it is used as evidence (yustadallu) for the demand. His saying: 'thus the primary aim is encompassed... etc.' – it is a branching from what preceded that its subject is conceptual and assertoric knowledge from the aspect... etc. His saying: 'the primary aim' – he guarded thereby against the secondary aim like the topic of expressions (1) and indications, for they are not intended by themselves in the art of logic, but are intended secondarily because definitions and syllogisms are composed from them. al-'Attar: His saying: 'thus the primary aim is encompassed' – it should be known first that the divisions of what leads to conceptual or assertoric knowledge investigated in logic are five: the near lead to conceptual, which are definitions (ma'arifat); the near lead to assertoric, which are proofs (hujaj); the far lead to conceptual, which is some of the five universals; the far lead to assertoric, which are propositions (qadaya); and the farthest lead to it, which are subjects, predicates, premises (muqaddimat), and consequents (tawali). And they did not mention regarding the lead to conceptual a farthest, and there is discussion (1). (His saying: 'like the topic of expressions... etc.') – it is better for him to say: like the principles of conceptuals, which are universals, and the principles of assertorics, which are propositions and their rulings, because they are intended secondarily; the primarily intended are the aims of conceptuals, which are explanatory statements, and the aims of assertorics, which are proofs. This is indicated by their restriction of the art to these four. As for the topic of expressions and indications, they are not part of the art at all. al-Sayyid said: it is better to make the topics of expressions also from the introduction. End. And by it he means the introduction of the book, and also indications. End. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines the terms hujja (proof) and dalil (evidence) and concludes the primary aim of logic. It begins by stating that what leads to an assertoric demand (matlub tasdiqi) is called hujja and dalil. Then it says that the primary aim of this art (logic) is encompassed by these two types: definitions (explanatory statements) for conceptuals and proofs for assertorics. The commentator al-Dusuqi explains that again, like with mu'arraf, the pronoun refers to the near lead, not any lead. He gives the etymology of hujja: it is called that because one who uses it to deduce his demand overcomes (hajja) his opponent, i.e., defeats him. Dalil is named because it is used as evidence (yustadallu bihi) for the demand. Then al-Dusuqi clarifies that the 'primary aim' excludes secondary aims like the study of expressions (alfaz) and indications (dalalat), which are not intended in themselves but only as means. Al-'Attar expands by enumerating the five types of leads (muwasil) investigated in logic: near to conceptual (definitions), near to assertoric (proofs), far to conceptual (some of the five universals), far to assertoric (propositions), and farthest to assertoric (subjects, predicates, premises, consequents). He notes that they did not mention a farthest lead to conceptual. He then criticizes the author (or commentator) for calling expressions and indications secondary; rather, he says the principles like universals and propositions are secondary, while the primary aims are the explanatory statements and proofs. He quotes al-Sayyid (al-Sharif al-Jurjani) that even expressions should be part of the introduction. This clarifies the scope of logic.

دَلِيل
مَقْصُود أَصْلِي

Essential Accident: Absolute and Oppositional Coverage
Definition of essential accident and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given accident is essential by checking if it covers all individuals of its subject absolutely or when paired with its opposite under contrast or privation/possession, and exclude affirmation/negation.

Core Idea
Essential accidents either cover universally or along with their opposite—but never with affirmation/negation.

In this passage, the author defines 'essential accident' (al-'arad al-dhati) with precision. He explains that an accident qualifies as essential if it covers all individuals of its subject either absolutely or when considered together with its opposite under contrast (al-taddād) or privation/possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka). The opposition of affirmation and negation is excluded because it is not specific to any concept. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly establishing the subject matter of a science and avoiding confusion about what counts as an essential accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of coverage
The author states that an essential accident must be comprehensive either absolutely (على الإطلاق) or when taken with its opposite (مع مقابلها) under the opposition of contrast or privation/possession. Locate these two types in the Arabic passage.
The opposite here refers to the contrary or the privation/possession pair, not just any negation.
2
Exclude affirmation/negation and understand why
The author explicitly excludes opposition of affirmation and negation (تقابل السلب والإيجاب) because it is not specific to any concept. For example, 'not-p' applies to everything that is not p, so it cannot define an accident specific to a subject. Reflect on why this exclusion is necessary for defining essential accidents.
Affirmation/negation would make every universal property essential, which is too broad.
3
Trace the hierarchical chain of essential accidents
The passage says essential accidents themselves have essential accidents, forming a hierarchy. This shows that scientific investigations ultimately return to essential accidents of the subject. For instance, in physics, the division of body into organic/inorganic is an essential accident, and further details are its restrictions.
This hierarchy justifies why all predicates in a science relate to the subject's essence.
4
Apply to an example from Islamic science
Take the subject 'body' in physics. The accident 'being natural or non-natural' is an essential accident because it covers all bodies absolutely. Verify that it meets the definition: it is comprehensive absolutely, not requiring an opposite. Check also a property like 'being organic/inorganic' — it covers by opposition under privation/possession (one has a soul, the other lacks it).
This example comes from the passage itself, showing how physics investigates essential accidents.

أفراد ذلك المفهوم على الإطلاق، أو مَعَ مُقابلة التّضاد أو العدمِ والمَلكة دونَ مقابلة السَّلْبِ والإيجاب؛ إذ المتقابِلَانِ تَقابُلَ الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ لا اختصاص لَهُما بمفهوم دونَ مَفهوم ضبطاً للانتشار بقدر الإمكان، فأثبتوا الأحوالَ الشَّامِلَةَ على الإطلاقِ لِنَفْسِ الموضوع، والشَّامِلةَ معَ مُقابلها لأنواعه واللاحقة للخارج المساوي لأعراضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ، ثمَّ إِنَّ تِلكَ العوارض الذَّاتِيَّةَ لَهَا عوارض ذائيَّةٌ شاملة لها على الإطلاقِ أو على التّقابل، فأثبتوا العوارضَ الشَّاملةَ على الإطلاقِ لِنَفْسِ الأعراض الذَّاتِيَّةِ والشَّاملة على التَّقابُلِ لأنواع تلكَ الأعراض، وكذلك عوارض تلك العوارض، وهذه العوارض في الحقيقة قيود للأعراض المثبتة للموضوعِ أو لأنواعِهِ، إلا أنَّها لِكَثرةِ مَبَاحثِها ; جُعِلَتْ مَحمولاتٍ على الأعراض، وهذا تفصيلُ ما قالُوا : معنى البحث عن الأعراض الذاتية أن تثبت تلك الأعراضُ لِنَفْسِ الموضوع أو لأنواعِهِ أو لأعراضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ أو لأنواعها أو أعراض أنواعها، وبِمَا ذكرْنَا اندفعَ مَا قِيْلَ إِنَّهُ مَا مِنْ عِلْمٍ إِلَّا ويبحث فيهِ عَن أحوالِهِ المختصَّةِ بأنواعِهِ، فيكون بحثاً عن الأعراض الغريبة للحوقها بواسطة أمرٍ أخص، كما يبحث في الطَّبيعيّ عَن الأحوال المختصة بالمعادنِ والنَّباتِ والحيوان، وذلك لأنَّ المبحوث عنه في الطَّبيعيّ؛ أنَّ الجسم إمَّا ذو طبيعة أو ذو نَفْسٍ، آلي أو غيرُ اليّ، وهِيَ مِن عوارضهِ الذَّاتية، والبحثُ عَن الأحوال المختصة بالعناصر وبالمركَّبَاتِ التَّامَّةِ أو غيرِ التَّامَّةِ كُلُّهَا تَفصيل لهذهِ العوارض وقيودٌ لَهَا .ا.هـ.
Individuals of that concept absolutely, or with the opposition of contrast or privation and possession, without the opposition of negation and affirmation — since the two opposites in the opposition of affirmation and negation have no specificity to any concept over another — to restrict diffusion as much as possible. So they established the states that are comprehensive absolutely of the subject itself, and the states that are comprehensive with their opposite for its types, and the states that follow what is external and equal to its essential accidents. Then those essential accidents have essential accidents that are comprehensive of them absolutely or by opposition. So they established the accidents that are comprehensive absolutely of the essential accidents themselves, and the accidents that are comprehensive by opposition for the types of those accidents, and likewise the accidents of those accidents. These accidents are in reality restrictions for the accidents established for the subject or its types, except that due to the abundance of their investigations, they were made predicates of the accidents. This is the detail of what they said: the meaning of investigating essential accidents is that those accidents are established for the subject itself, or for its types, or for its essential accidents, or for their types, or the accidents of their types. And by what we have mentioned, the objection is refuted that there is no science except that it investigates its specific states of its types, so it would be an investigation of alien accidents because they follow by means of something more specific, as in physics one investigates states specific to minerals, plants, and animals. That is because what is investigated in physics is that a body is either endowed with nature or endowed with a soul, organic or inorganic, and these are among its essential accidents. The investigation of states specific to elements and complete or incomplete compounds are all details of these accidents and restrictions for them. End quote.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage defines 'arad dhati (essential accident) and explains its types. The author states that an essential accident must be either universal ('ala al-itlaq) in covering all individuals of the subject, or covered when taken with its opposite (muqābil) under the opposition of contrast (al-taḍād) or privation and possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka). The opposition of affirmation and negation (salb wa ījāb) is excluded because it is not specific to any concept. For example, 'every body is either moving or at rest' uses contrasting opposites, while 'every number is either even or odd' uses privation and possession. The author then explains that essential accidents themselves have essential accidents, and these form a hierarchy. The passage refutes a claim that in every science we investigate specific states of types, which would be alien accidents (a'rāḍ gharība). The author argues that such states are actually details and restrictions of essential accidents. For instance, in physics, the division of body into natural/non-natural or organic/inorganic are essential accidents, and the study of elements and compounds is a detail of these. This shows that all investigations in a science ultimately relate to essential accidents of its subject.

Key Terms
العرض الذاتي
تقابل التضاد
تقابل العدم والملكة

Key Takeaway
An essential accident must either cover all individuals of its subject absolutely or, when paired with its opposite, under contrast or privation/possession — never under affirmation/negation.

Two Modes of Essential Accident Comprehensiveness
Commentator clarifies the two modes of essential accident comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the individual and opposition-based modes of comprehensiveness for essential accidents and provide Islamic science examples for each.

Core Idea
An essential accident covers all individuals either alone or jointly with its opposite.

Al-Dasooqi refines the definition of the essential accident by clarifying a key condition: its comprehensiveness of all individuals of the subject. He explains that this comprehensiveness can occur either individually, like 'occupying space' for body, or through a pair of opposites, like 'straight or curved' for lines. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly identifying essential predicates in Islamic sciences like Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of essential accident
An essential accident is a predicate that is necessary for the subject and comprehensive of all its individuals. Al-Dasooqi builds on this foundation.
2
Identify the two modes of comprehensiveness
In the passage, al-Dasooqi states that comprehensiveness can be either 'ala al-infiraad (individually) or 'ala sabeel al-taqaabul (by way of opposition).
The Arabic terms are key: الانفراد means alone, التقابل means opposition.
3
Analyze the individual mode example
Consider 'kullu mutaḥayyiz' (every occupier of space). The predicate 'occupying space' alone covers all bodies without needing its opposite.
This is typical of many essential accidents in Kalam, like 'temporally originated' for bodies.
4
Analyze the opposition mode example
For 'every line is either straight or curved', neither straight nor curved individually covers all lines, but together they do. Similarly for number (even/odd).
The two opposites together form a disjunction that covers the entire subject.
5
Distinguish from invalid opposition types
Al-Dasooqi notes that affirmation/negation (e.g., possible/not possible) does not work because such opposites apply to multiple subjects, not just one science.
This sets the stage for the next lesson on valid opposition types.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو كلامٌ مُحرَّر؛ إِلَّا أَنَّ فِيهِ خفاءٌ نُوضحه لك، وهو أنَّ معنى قولِهِ : سواءٌ كَانَ شَامِلاً لجميع أفراد ذلك المفهوم . . . إلخ؛ انه اعتبر في العَرَضِ الذَّاتي شموله لجميع أفراد الموضوع؛ إمَّا على الانفرادِ أو على سبيل التَّقابُل؛ فكلّ محمولات المسائل مع مقابلاتها؛ أعني : محمولات المسائل الأُخر، شَامِلٌ لِجميع أفرادِ موضوعِ العِلْمِ، فيكونُ عَرَضاً ذاتيا له، مثال شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتي على سبيل الانفراد : كُلُّ متحيز، فإنَّ التَّحيز وَحدَهُ شَامِلٌ لِجَميعِ أفرادِ الجِسْمِ، بدونِ أنْ يعتبر معهُ مقابلُهُ أو شمولهُ معَ مُقابِلِهِ، بمعنى أنَّهُ إذا لُوحِةً وحدَهُ لا يَكونُ شَامِلاً، فإنْ لُوحِظَ مع مُقابِلِهِ تحقَّقَ الشُّمولُ؛ سواء كانَ التَّقابل بينَهُ وبينَ ذلك المقابل تقابلَ الضَّدَّيْنِ، أَو تَقَابُلَ العَدَمِ والمَلَكَةِ، مثالُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيِّ الشَّامِلِ على سبيلِ التَّقابُلِ قولُنَا : كُلُّ خَطِّ إِمَّا مُنحَنِ وَإِمَّا مُستقيم، فالتَّقابُلُ بينَ الاستقامة والانحناء تقابلُ التَّضاد، ولا شَكٌّ أَنَّ مَجموع الأمرَيْنِ عَرَضٌ شامل لجميع أفرادِ الخطوط، وأما الاستقامة وحدها أو الانحناء وحدَهُ؛ فَلَا، وَمِثَالُ العَرَضِ الذَاتيّ الشَّامل على سبيل تقابل العدم والملكة : العدد إمَّا زَوج أو فرد، فالتقابل بين الفرديَّةِ والزَّوجيَّةِ تَقابل العدم والمَلَكَةِ، ولا شَكٍّ في شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيّ في هَذَينِ المثالينِ لِلمَوضوع؛ مع اعتبارِ التَّقابل لا أحدهما فقط، وأمَّا التَّقابل على طريقِ السَّلْبِ والإيجابِ؛ فَغَيرُ مُعتبرٍ لِمَا قالَ؛ إذ المتقابلان تقابل الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ لا اختصاص لهما بمفهوم دون مفهوم؛ لُنَا : الجوهرُ إمَّا مُمكن أوْ لا؛ ليس فيه شمول؛ لأنَّ كُلًّا مِنَ الإمكانِ وسَلبِهِ لا يختصَّان بالجوهر، إذ يجريان في العَرَضِ أيضاً، والحاصل أنَّنا نعتبر في المتقابلين على الوجه المذكورِ أنْ يكونَ كُلَّ واحدٍ مِنهُما مَحمُولاً مع ما يقابلهُ إذا أخذ على وجهِ التَّرديدِ كالأمثلة المذكورة.
This is well-composed speech, except that there is obscurity in it which we clarify for you. It is that the meaning of his statement: 'whether it is comprehensive of all individuals of that concept... etc.' is that he considered in the essential accident its comprehensiveness of all individuals of the subject; either individually or by way of opposition. So all the predicates of the problems with their opposites — meaning the predicates of the other problems — are comprehensive of all individuals of the subject of the science, so it is an essential accident for it. An example of the comprehensiveness of the essential accident individually: every occupier of space (kullu mutaḥayyiz), for occupying space alone is comprehensive of all individuals of body, without considering its opposite or its comprehensiveness with its opposite. An example of the essential accident comprehensive by way of opposition is our statement: every line is either curved or straight. The opposition between straightness and curvature is the opposition of contrast, and there is no doubt that the combination of the two is an accident comprehensive of all individuals of lines. As for straightness alone or curvature alone, it is not. An example of the essential accident comprehensive by way of the opposition of privation and possession: number is either even or odd. The opposition between oddness and evenness is the opposition of privation and possession. There is no doubt that the essential accident in these two examples is comprehensive of the subject, considering the opposition, not just one of them. As for opposition by way of negation and affirmation, it is not considered for what he said: the two opposites in the opposition of affirmation and negation have no specificity to any concept over another. For example, substance is either possible or not; there is no comprehensiveness in it because both possibility and its negation are not specific to substance, since they apply to accident as well. The conclusion is that we consider in the two opposites in the mentioned manner that each one of them be predicated together with its opposite when taken on the mode of disjunction, like the examples mentioned.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by al-Dasooqi clarifying the earlier definition of 'arad dhati. He explains that the condition of the essential accident's comprehensiveness can occur either individually (e.g., 'every body occupies space' — tahayyuz alone covers all bodies) or through a pair of opposites (e.g., 'every line is either straight or curved' — neither alone covers all lines, but together they do). The opposition can be of contrast (taḍād) or privation/possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka). Al-Dasooqi emphasizes that affirmation/negation opposition (e.g., 'every substance is either possible or not possible') is invalid because such opposites apply to all categories, not specifically to a given subject. Thus, for an accident to be essential, the pair of opposites must be specific to that subject, such as even/odd for numbers or curved/straight for lines.

على سبيل التقابل
العرض الذاتي

Key Takeaway
An essential accident can cover all individuals either by a single predicate or through a disjunction of opposites specific to the subject.

Valid Types of Opposition for Essential Accidents
Commentator specifies valid opposition types for essential accidents and excludes affirmation/negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of opposition (contrast, privation/possession) are valid for essential accidents and explain why affirmation/negation is excluded using al-Dasooqi's reasoning.

Core Idea
Only opposition of contrast or privation/possession can ground an essential accident; affirmation/negation fails because it is not subject-specific.

Al-Dasooqi further refines the opposition mode by specifying which types of opposition are valid. He explicitly rules out the opposition of affirmation and negation, using the example 'substance is either possible or not'. Understanding why this pair fails deepens our grasp of how essential accidents are tied to the subject's essence in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of opposition
Recall from the passage: al-Dasooqi mentions opposition of contrast (taḍād), privation/possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka), and affirmation/negation (al-ījāb wa al-salb).
These are standard categories in Islamic logic.
2
Examine the validity of contrast and privation/possession
For lines, straight vs curved is contrast. For numbers, even vs odd is privation/possession. Both types yield pairs that are specific to their subjects: only lines can be straight or curved; only numbers can be even or odd.
Al-Dasooqi states these are valid.
3
Analyze why affirmation/negation is invalid
Consider 'substance is either possible or not possible'. The pair 'possible' and 'not possible' applies to all categories (substance, accident, etc.), not just substance. Thus, it lacks specificity and cannot be an essential accident of substance.
The argument is that essential accidents must be proper to the subject's concept.
4
Formulate the logical reasoning
Al-Dasooqi implicitly uses a syllogism: Essential accidents must be specific to their subject. Affirmation/negation pairs (like possible/not possible) are not specific. Therefore, such pairs cannot be essential accidents.
This reasoning clarifies the condition for the opposition mode.
5
Apply the criterion to new examples
Given a pair like 'corporeal or spiritual', check if it is specific to substance. If it applies only to substance, it would be valid; if it applies also to accident (e.g., spiritual accident? No, but 'corporeal' is specific to bodies).
Practice ensures mastery.

وهو كلامٌ مُحرَّر؛ إِلَّا أَنَّ فِيهِ خفاءٌ نُوضحه لك، وهو أنَّ معنى قولِهِ : سواءٌ كَانَ شَامِلاً لجميع أفراد ذلك المفهوم . . . إلخ؛ انه اعتبر في العَرَضِ الذَّاتي شموله لجميع أفراد الموضوع؛ إمَّا على الانفرادِ أو على سبيل التَّقابُل؛ فكلّ محمولات المسائل مع مقابلاتها؛ أعني : محمولات المسائل الأُخر، شَامِلٌ لِجميع أفرادِ موضوعِ العِلْمِ، فيكونُ عَرَضاً ذاتيا له، مثال شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتي على سبيل الانفراد : كُلُّ متحيز، فإنَّ التَّحيز وَحدَهُ شَامِلٌ لِجَميعِ أفرادِ الجِسْمِ، بدونِ أنْ يعتبر معهُ مقابلُهُ أو شمولهُ معَ مُقابِلِهِ، بمعنى أنَّهُ إذا لُوحِةً وحدَهُ لا يَكونُ شَامِلاً، فإنْ لُوحِظَ مع مُقابِلِهِ تحقَّقَ الشُّمولُ؛ سواء كانَ التَّقابل بينَهُ وبينَ ذلك المقابل تقابلَ الضَّدَّيْنِ، أَو تَقَابُلَ العَدَمِ والمَلَكَةِ، مثالُ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيِّ الشَّامِلِ على سبيلِ التَّقابُلِ قولُنَا : كُلُّ خَطِّ إِمَّا مُنحَنِ وَإِمَّا مُستقيم، فالتَّقابُلُ بينَ الاستقامة والانحناء تقابلُ التَّضاد، ولا شَكٌّ أَنَّ مَجموع الأمرَيْنِ عَرَضٌ شامل لجميع أفرادِ الخطوط، وأما الاستقامة وحدها أو الانحناء وحدَهُ؛ فَلَا، وَمِثَالُ العَرَضِ الذَاتيّ الشَّامل على سبيل تقابل العدم والملكة : العدد إمَّا زَوج أو فرد، فالتقابل بين الفرديَّةِ والزَّوجيَّةِ تَقابل العدم والمَلَكَةِ، ولا شَكٍّ في شمولِ العَرَضِ الذَّاتيّ في هَذَينِ المثالينِ لِلمَوضوع؛ مع اعتبارِ التَّقابل لا أحدهما فقط، وأمَّا التَّقابل على طريقِ السَّلْبِ والإيجابِ؛ فَغَيرُ مُعتبرٍ لِمَا قالَ؛ إذ المتقابلان تقابل الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ لا اختصاص لهما بمفهوم دون مفهوم؛ لُنَا : الجوهرُ إمَّا مُمكن أوْ لا؛ ليس فيه شمول؛ لأنَّ كُلًّا مِنَ الإمكانِ وسَلبِهِ لا يختصَّان بالجوهر، إذ يجريان في العَرَضِ أيضاً، والحاصل أنَّنا نعتبر في المتقابلين على الوجه المذكورِ أنْ يكونَ كُلَّ واحدٍ مِنهُما مَحمُولاً مع ما يقابلهُ إذا أخذ على وجهِ التَّرديدِ كالأمثلة المذكورة.
This is well-composed speech, except that there is obscurity in it which we clarify for you. It is that the meaning of his statement: 'whether it is comprehensive of all individuals of that concept... etc.' is that he considered in the essential accident its comprehensiveness of all individuals of the subject; either individually or by way of opposition. So all the predicates of the problems with their opposites — meaning the predicates of the other problems — are comprehensive of all individuals of the subject of the science, so it is an essential accident for it. An example of the comprehensiveness of the essential accident individually: every occupier of space (kullu mutaḥayyiz), for occupying space alone is comprehensive of all individuals of body, without considering its opposite or its comprehensiveness with its opposite. An example of the essential accident comprehensive by way of opposition is our statement: every line is either curved or straight. The opposition between straightness and curvature is the opposition of contrast, and there is no doubt that the combination of the two is an accident comprehensive of all individuals of lines. As for straightness alone or curvature alone, it is not. An example of the essential accident comprehensive by way of the opposition of privation and possession: number is either even or odd. The opposition between oddness and evenness is the opposition of privation and possession. There is no doubt that the essential accident in these two examples is comprehensive of the subject, considering the opposition, not just one of them. As for opposition by way of negation and affirmation, it is not considered for what he said: the two opposites in the opposition of affirmation and negation have no specificity to any concept over another. For example, substance is either possible or not; there is no comprehensiveness in it because both possibility and its negation are not specific to substance, since they apply to accident as well. The conclusion is that we consider in the two opposites in the mentioned manner that each one of them be predicated together with its opposite when taken on the mode of disjunction, like the examples mentioned.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by al-Dasooqi clarifying the earlier definition of 'arad dhati. He explains that the condition of the essential accident's comprehensiveness can occur either individually (e.g., 'every body occupies space' — tahayyuz alone covers all bodies) or through a pair of opposites (e.g., 'every line is either straight or curved' — neither alone covers all lines, but together they do). The opposition can be of contrast (taḍād) or privation/possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka). Al-Dasooqi emphasizes that affirmation/negation opposition (e.g., 'every substance is either possible or not possible') is invalid because such opposites apply to all categories, not specifically to a given subject. Thus, for an accident to be essential, the pair of opposites must be specific to that subject, such as even/odd for numbers or curved/straight for lines.

Key Terms
تقابل التضاد
تقابل العدم والملكة
تقابل الإيجاب والسلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Dasooqi consider the pair 'possible/not possible' invalid for defining substance?
A) Because possibility and its negation are not opposites.
B) Because they are not comprehensive of all substances.
C) Because they also apply to accidents, so they are not specific to substance.
D) Because substance is not possible.

Key Takeaway
For an opposition pair to form an essential accident, both members must be specific to the subject; affirmation/negation fails this test.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Accidental Accident (al-Arad al-Gharīb)
Defines accidental accident and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of accidental accidents (by specific, general, or disparate cause) and distinguish them from essential accidents using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The accidental accident attaches via a cause that is more specific, more general, or disparate from its subject.

In classical Islamic logic, definitions are central. Here, al-Dasuqi defines a key type of accident that is not essential but accidental in a special sense. Understanding this distinction helps you evaluate whether a given attribute is essential or accidental to a subject, crucial for constructing sound definitions and arguments in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum, 'al-‘arad al-gharīb' (the accidental accident). Note that it is a type of accident (عَرَض) that attaches to a subject via a cause.
Contrast with essential accident (عرض ذاتي) defined earlier.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'accident' (a dependent attribute). The differentia is that it attaches 'by means of a cause that is more specific, more general, or disparate' from the subject.
Three distinct modes: a cause more specific (أخص), more general (أعم), or disparate (مباين).
3
Analyse the examples
Laughter attaches to animal by being human (more specific); motion attaches to man by being animal (more general); color attaches to body via surface (disparate). Check each example to see which type it represents.
The example of 'color attaching via surface' likely refers to surface (السطح) as a disparate cause.
4
Compare with essential accidents
Essential accidents (like occupying space for body) do not require a separate cause; they follow from the essence. Accidental accidents require a mediating cause.
This distinction is foundational for definitions in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعَرَضُ الغريب؛ ما يكون لحوقه للمعروض بواسطة أمرٍ أخصَّ؛ كَالضّحك اللاحق للحيوان بواسطة كونه إنساناً، وهو أخص أو أعم ؛ كَالتَّحَرُكِ اللَّاحِقِ لِلإِنسَانِ بواسطة كونه حيواناً ، أو مُباين لهُ ؛ كَاللُّونِ العارض للجسم بواسطة الشطح، وكالحرارة العطار الدَّرَّاكة وغير ذلك، وتارة يكونُ غير الي كالنَّباتِ ؛ فإنَّهم أثبتوا لهُ نَفْساً، وقَوْلُهُ : وبالمركبات الثامة أو غيرِ الثَّامَةِ؛ لا تتوهَّمْ أنَّ المراد بها المركَّبات في الأقوال؛ بل المركَّباتُ مِنَ العناصِرِ ، وهِيَ المولّداتُ الثَّلاثُ، أعني: الحيوان والمعدن والنَّبات؛ فَإِنَّهُم فَسَّمُوا المركَّبَ إلى تام وغير تام وشرح ذلك مع إثباتِ أنَّ لِلنَّبات نَفْساً مِمَّا يطولُ بِهِ الكلامُ، فَليطلبُ مِنَ الكتب الحكميَّة، وقد أشبعنا فيه القول في شَرْحِنَا لِنزهة الأذهان في علم الطّب، وبقي جوابان آخران عن الإشكال؛ الأول: أنَّه يجوز أن يكون البحث في المعلوم عن الأحوال المختصَّةِ بأنواع موضوعِ العِلْمِ واقعاً على سبيل التَّطقُلِ الثَّاني : أنَّه يجوز أنْ يكونَ البحث عنها راجعاً إلى البحث عن الأحوال المشتركةِ الَّتِي هِيَ أعراضُ الذَّاتِيَّةِ لِمَوضوعاتِ العلوم لتضمنها إيَّاها استطراداً وتبعاً لا أصالة قال أبو الفتح وهَذَانِ الاحتمالانِ، وإِنْ كَانَا غيرَ ظَاهِرَيْن، لكنَّ ضَمَّ النَّشرِ أحسنُ فَلِلمتأخّريْنَ أن يرتكبوا أحدَ التَّأويلين ترجيحاً لِضَمّ النَّشْرِ، ثمَّ لا بُدَّ مِنَ التَّبرُّعِ لَكَ بفائدة جليلةٍ يَتَّضِحُ لَكَ بِهَا قولُ عبد الحكيم : إنَّهُ يلزم حينئذٍ دخولُ العِلْمِ الجزئي في العِلْمِ الكُلّيّ، وبيان ذلكَ أنَّ العِلْمَ الطَّبيعي باحث عَن الأجسام الطَّبيعيَّةِ مِنْ حيثُ هِيَ والجسمُ بِهذه الحيثيَّةِ كُلّيّ، تحتَهُ أنواع كَالكُرَةِ مَثَلاً نَوعٌ منه ، وكَوْنُ تِلكَ الكُرَةِ مُتحرّكَةً ؛ نَوعٌ مِن مُطلق كرةٍ، فَالعوارِضُ اللَّاحِقَّةُ لِلجسمِ مِن حيثُ هُوَ جِسمٌ يحملُ على موضوعِ العِلْمِ الطَّبيعيّ وهُوَ الجِسْمُ من حيثُ هُوَ، والعوارضُ اللاحقة باعتبارِ كَوْنِهِ كُرَةً؛ تُحملُ على ذلكَ الجزئي
And the accidental accident is that which attaches to the subject by means of a more specific cause, like laughter attaching to animal by virtue of being human – and it may be more specific or more general, like motion attaching to man by virtue of being animal, or disparate, like color attaching to body by virtue of [؟] and heat. Al-Attar: [؟] and other things, and sometimes it is non-instrumental like plants, for they have established a soul for it. And his statement: 'And by perfect or imperfect compounds' – do not think that by them are meant compounds in statements; rather, compounds from the elements, which are the three generables: animal, mineral, and plant. For they divided the compound into perfect and imperfect, and an explanation of that with proof that plants have a soul is lengthy; so let it be sought from philosophical books. And we have given complete discourse on it in our commentary on Nuzhat al-Adhhan in medicine. And there remain two other answers to the objection: First, that it is permissible for the investigation in the known sciences about the states specific to the types of the subject to occur in the manner of [؟]. Second, that it is permissible for the investigation about them to refer back to the investigation about the common states that are essential accidents of the subjects of the sciences, as they include them incidentally and subordinately, not essentially. Abu al-Fath said: And these two possibilities, even if they are not apparent, but the inclusion of publication is better, so for the later scholars they may adopt one of the two interpretations, preferring the inclusion of publication. Then there must be given to you a noble benefit by which the statement of 'Abd al-Hakim becomes clear: that at that time the entrance of the partial science into the universal science follows, and the explanation of that is that natural science investigates natural bodies insofar as they are, and body in this respect is universal, under it are types like sphere for example, and that sphere's being moving is a type of absolute sphere. So the accidents that attach to body insofar as it is body are predicated of the subject of natural science, which is body insofar as it is, and the accidents that attach by virtue of its being a sphere are predicated of that partial.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines accidental accidents (al-‘araḍ al-gharīb) and then branches into several related topics. First, al-Dasuqi defines the accidental accident as one that attaches to its subject by means of a cause that is either more specific (e.g., laughter attaching to animal via being human), more general (e.g., motion attaching to man via being animal), or disparate (e.g., color attaching to body via surface? and heat). Then al-Attar comments, clarifying that 'perfect and imperfect compounds' refer to natural compounds (mineral, plant, animal) not linguistic compounds. He also notes that plants have a soul, referring the reader to his medical commentary. Next, he presents two answers to an objection about the investigation of specific states in sciences: either the investigation is done in the manner of [؟], or it reduces to the investigation of common essential accidents incidentally. These answers are attributed to Abu al-Fath, who admits they are not apparent but may be used to justify publication. Finally, a benefit from 'Abd al-Hakim explains that partial sciences enter into universal sciences: natural science studies body universally, but specific bodies like spheres have their own accidents, which are partial – so the study of those partial accidents belongs to partial sciences under the universal science.

Key Terms
العَرَض الغريب
الأخص
الأعم
المباين
